**Main problem.** Coherence times in tantalum-based superconducting quantum circuits are limited by dielectric losses from two-level systems (TLS) at the metal-air and substrate-air interfaces, which are worsened by native oxide regrowth.

**Main result.** Applying an all-surface organic monolayer (SAM) passivation using 1-octadecene increases the internal quality factor of tantalum resonators by approximately 140% in the single-photon regime at 100 mK.

**Method.** The researchers used chemical vapor/liquid-phase thermal activation to graft alkene-based self-assembled monolayers onto etched tantalum and silicon, followed by extensive surface characterization (XPS, TEM, AFM) and cryogenic microwave spectroscopy.

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates a method to improve the performance of tantalum superconducting resonators by using organic monolayers to passivate all surfaces. By preventing the regrowth of lossy native oxides, the researchers achieved a 140% increase in the internal quality factor in the single-photon regime. The study confirms that the passivation effectively reduces two-level system (TLS) losses without altering bulk properties. This approach offers a promising route for developing high-coherence components for superconducting quantum computers.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Model / system.** The experimental platform consists of alpha-phase tantalum coplanar waveguide (CPW) resonators deposited on high-resistivity silicon (100) substrates, operating in the 5-9 GHz range at millikelvin temperatures.

**Key observables.** Internal quality factor (Qi), TLS-induced loss (QTLS), quasiparticle loss (QQP), power-independent loss (Qother), and fractional frequency shift.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Temperature (100 mK to 900 mK), microwave frequency (5-9 GHz), mean photon number, and surface roughness (RMS).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study assumes that the observed enhancement is primarily due to the reduction of TLS-induced losses rather than changes to bulk superconducting properties.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the passivation schematic and growth mechanisms; Figure 2 compares surface wettability; Figure 5 illustrates frequency shifts vs. temperature; Figure 9 shows the extraction of various loss parameters (TLS, QP, and other); Figure 10 shows loss weights vs. temperature.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of interface loss, describes the SAM fabrication and growth mechanism, details the surface characterization techniques to confirm passivation, presents microwave performance measurements (power and temperature dependence), and concludes with an analysis of the underlying loss mechanisms.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant for researchers in quantum optics and open quantum systems as it provides a scalable material-science solution to mitigate environmental decoherence (TLS) in superconducting hardware.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Tantalum is a promising platform for superconducting quantum circuits, yet coherence times remain limited by dielectric losses from interfacial two-level systems (TLS), exacerbated by native oxide regrowth. Here, we implement molecular surface passivation using self-assembled organic monolayers on freshly etched tantalum and silicon in coplanar waveguide resonators. Surface characterization by contact angle, XPS, FTIR and TEM confirm the formation of ordered, nanometer-thick films that suppress oxide formation. Microwave measurements in the ~5-9 GHz range reveal internal quality factors up to 1.8x10^6 in the single-photon regime at 100 mK, representing a ~140% improvement over untreated devices with native oxide. Power and temperature dependent measurements attribute this enhancement to reduced TLS-induced losses. These results demonstrate that molecular passivation effectively engineers low-loss interfaces and provides a scalable route toward high-coherence superconducting quantum devices.

</details>

**Main problem.** Investigating how a periodic electric field drive modifies the mobility edge structure and transport properties in the generalized Aubry-Andre (GPD) model.

**Main result.** The study uncovers two distinct Floquet mobility edges (delocalized-localized and multifractal-localized) and demonstrates that driving parameters can control transport regimes ranging from superdiffusive to subdiffusive.

**Method.** The authors use Floquet-Magnus expansion and Avila's global theory for analytical predictions, alongside numerical diagnostics like fractal dimension, IPR, and real-time wave packet dynamics.

**Summary.** This paper explores how a periodic electric field can be used to tune the localization properties of a quasiperiodic system. The authors identify two types of Floquet mobility edges depending on the model's deformation parameter. They show that the driving amplitude can induce full localization at specific points and that the driving frequency can switch transport between superdiffusive and subdiffusive regimes. This work highlights the potential for using periodic driving to precisely engineer quantum transport in disordered systems.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Model / system.** A one-dimensional periodically driven Ganeshan-Pixley-Das Sarma (GPD) model describing non-interacting spinless fermions on a quasiperiodic lattice subject to a time-periodic electric field.

**Key observables.** Fractal dimension (D2), Inverse Participation Ratio (IPR), Lyapunov exponent, root-mean-squared deviation (RMSD), and return probability.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Driving amplitude (K), driving frequency (omega), potential strength (lambda), and the deformation parameter (beta).

**Assumptions / limitations.** High-frequency limit approximations are used for the effective Hamiltonian; higher-order terms in the Floquet-Magnus expansion are neglected in certain analytical derivations.

**Figures summary.** Figures show the fractal dimension as a function of eigenenergy and driving strength, the scaling of IPR to extract fractal dimensions, and the time evolution of return probability and wave packet spreading.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the driven GPD model, applies Floquet-Magnus expansion and Avila's theory to identify mobility edges, analyzes spectral properties (fractal dimension/IPR), investigates transport dynamics (RMSD/diffusion), and discusses frequency-dependent deviations.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a powerful mechanism for engineering mobility edges and controlling transport via external driving, which is highly relevant for controlling localization in driven many-body systems and synthetic quantum lattices.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate the effect of a periodic electric field drive on the generalized Aubry-André model, also known as the Ganeshan-Pixley-Das Sarma (GPD) model, which is well known as a host of mobility edges. Our study of the Floquet spectrum of the driven GPD model uncovers the emergence of two distinct Floquet mobility edges, a delocalized--localized (DL) edge in the bounded regime, and a multifractal--localized (ML) edge in the unbounded regime. Using analytical results derived from Avila's global theory applied to the high frequency effective Hamiltonian, together with numerical diagnostics such as the fractal dimension and inverse participation ratio, we demonstrate that these mobility edges can be effectively controlled by the amplitude and frequency of the electric field drive. We also identify drive-induced localization at specific values of the driving parameters, corresponding to dynamical localization points in the absence of quasiperiodic potential. Furthermore, the dynamical study of the periodically driven GPD model demonstrates superdiffusive to almost ballistic transport in the bounded regime corresponding to the DL edges, whereas subdiffusive transport is observed in the unbounded regime associated with the ML edges. We also analyze deviations from the high-frequency effective description by explicitly examining the low-frequency driving regime, where significant and counterintuitive deviations in both spectral properties and transport behavior are observed. Our study highlights the interplay of a quasiperiodic potential and a periodically varying electric field drive as a powerful mechanism to engineer mobility edges and control transport in systems with rich spectral features.

</details>

**Main problem.** Developing a quantum-enhanced sensing scheme to detect extremely weak signals from wave-like dark matter (axion-like particles, dark photons) and high-frequency gravitational waves.

**Main result.** The proposed protocol achieves super-Heisenberg scaling in sensitivity relative to the number of ions and provides a promising platform for exploring previously inaccessible parameter spaces for light dark matter and gravitational waves.

**Method.** The protocol uses an adiabatic evolution via a Tavis-Cummings Hamiltonian to prepare spin-motion squeezed states, followed by optical dipole force operations to transduce motional excitations into measurable spin signals.

**Summary.** This paper proposes a new quantum sensing protocol using 2D ion crystals in Penning traps to detect wave-like dark matter and high-frequency gravitational waves. By preparing spin-motion squeezed states through an adiabatic process, the researchers demonstrate a 'super-Heisenberg' scaling of sensitivity. The study evaluates the protocol's performance against realistic experimental noise like dephasing. Ultimately, the method offers a way to probe previously unexplored frequency and coupling ranges for fundamental physics.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Model / system.** The system consists of two-dimensional ion crystals (specifically Be+ ions) in a Penning trap, where collective phonon modes (drumhead and center-of-mass modes) are coupled to the ions' internal qubit states via spin-motion coupling.

**Key observables.** The expectation value and variance of the collective spin z-component (Jz), and the resulting phase sensitivity.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Number of ions (N), squeezing parameter (r), dephasing rate (Gamma_dep), decoherence rate (Gamma_dec), and dark matter mass/frequency.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis is limited to N < 100 and r < 2; it assumes a classical wave nature for dark matter and neglects finite temperature or frequency detuning errors.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows signal scaling with N; Figure 2 shows variance bounds; Figures 3-4 show expectation values and sensitivity vs N; Figure 5 illustrates the transition from super-Heisenberg to Heisenberg scaling; Figure 6 shows sensitivity vs squeezing parameter; Figure 7 shows the impact of dephasing.

**Paper structure.** Introduction of the sensing problem, description of the 2D ion crystal platform and Hamiltonian, detailed protocol for state preparation and sensing, sensitivity analysis for dark matter and gravitational waves, evaluation of noise/decoherence effects, and conclusion.

**Why it may be interesting.** It combines many-body dynamics (ion crystals), quantum control (adiabatic state preparation), and open quantum systems (dephasing/decoherence) to address fundamental questions in particle physics and gravitation.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We propose a quantum-enhanced sensing scheme for the detection of wave-like dark matter and high-frequency gravitational waves using two-dimensional ion crystals in a Penning trap. The protocol employs spin-motion squeezed states to improve the signal-to-noise ratio and enable a super-Heisenberg scaling with respect to the number of ions over a broad parameter range. We analyze the sensitivity of the protocol to representative wave-like dark matter candidates, including the axion-like particle and the dark photon, as well as to high-frequency gravitational waves, taking into account the decoherence and dephasing of the ion spins. Our results indicate that two-dimensional ion crystals and this new protocol provide a promising platform for probing previously unexplored parameter space in searches for light dark matter and high-frequency gravitational waves.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.22421"></a>
### [Two flavor neutrino oscillations in presence of non-Hermitian dynamics](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.22421v1)

**Authors:** Kritika Rushiya, Gaurav Hajong, Bhabani Prasad Mandal, Poonam Mehta  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.22421v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `dissipative systems` **4/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>

**Main problem.** Developing a consistent mathematical framework for two-flavor neutrino oscillations in the presence of non-Hermitian dynamics, specifically addressing the failure of conventional inner products to preserve probability.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that the G-metric approach fails to conserve probability in both PT-unbroken and PT-broken regimes, whereas the Brody and Graefe density matrix prescription ensures probability conservation and reveals non-Markovian behavior.

**Method.** The study compares two approaches: a bi-orthonormal inner product using a positive-definite metric operator G and the Brody and Graefe density matrix prescription involving a trace-preserving master equation.

**Summary.** This paper investigates how to mathematically model neutrino oscillations when the system is governed by non-Hermitian dynamics. The authors find that standard methods for defining inner products fail to conserve probability in PT-symmetric systems. Instead, they propose using the Brody and Graefe density matrix prescription, which maintains probability conservation and reveals that the system exhibits non-Markovian behavior in the steady state.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Model / system.** A two-flavor neutrino oscillation model in the ultra-relativistic limit, using a non-Hermitian Hamiltonian that includes a standard Hermitian part for vacuum oscillations and a non-Hermitian part characterized by parameters kappa, sigma, phi, and chi.

**Key observables.** Neutrino flavor transition probabilities (Paa, Pab, Pba, Pbb) and probability conservation.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Mass squared difference (delta m^2), mixing angle (theta), non-Hermitian parameters (kappa, sigma, phi, chi), and the PT-unbroken vs. PT-broken regimes.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Neutrinos are treated in the ultra-relativistic limit with equal fixed momenta/energy.

**Figures summary.** Figure 2 shows probability non-conservation in the G-metric approach; Figure 3 shows conserved probabilities using the density matrix prescription; Figure 4 illustrates non-Markovian behavior in the PT-symmetric case.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of non-Hermitian neutrino oscillations, defines the Hamiltonian and symmetry regimes, compares the G-metric and density matrix approaches, presents analytical results for probabilities, and discusses the implications of non-Markovianity.

**Why it may be interesting.** This paper is highly relevant to researchers in open quantum systems as it addresses fundamental issues in defining unitary-like evolution and probability conservation in non-Hermitian and PT-symmetric dynamics.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We develop a consistent mathematical framework for studying two flavor neutrino oscillations in presence of non-Hermitian dynamics. We consider two approaches : (a) bi-orthonormal inner product defined by a positive-definite metric operator $\mathcal{G}$ and (b) the density matrix prescription by Brody and Graefe [Phys. Rev. Lett. 109, 230405 (2012)]. For the $\mathcal{PT}$-symmetric case, we show that the $\mathcal{G}$ metric approach does not work well (probabilities are not conserved) both in $\mathcal{PT}$-unbroken as well as $\mathcal{PT}$-broken regime. Hence, we adopt the density matrix prescription by Brody and Graefe which is a positive semi-definite map. In the density matrix prescription, we note that probability in the steady state limit is not necessarily $1/2$ thereby indicating non-Markovian behavior.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.22481"></a>
### [Double Slit Experiment in the Heisenberg Picture of Quantum Mechanics](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.22481v1)

**Authors:** Vlatko Vedral  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.22481v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `quantum measurements` **4/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.22481_figures/2604.22481_page2.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Low-resolution page preview, page 2</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.22481_figures/2604.22481_page3.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> Low-resolution page preview, page 3</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the misconception that the double-slit experiment implies non-locality in quantum mechanics.

**Main result.** The author demonstrates that interference fringes can be fully explained through local interactions by treating position and momentum observables as space-time dependent fields within the Heisenberg picture.

**Method.** The study utilizes the Heisenberg picture of quantum mechanics, employing commutator analysis and the 'Church of the Larger Hilbert Space' to treat measurements as local entanglement between the particle and the screen.

**Summary.** This paper re-examines the double-slit experiment using the Heisenberg picture to argue against the necessity of invoking non-locality. By treating position and momentum as space-time dependent fields, the author shows that interference can be understood through local interactions. The work also explores the 'Church of the Larger Hilbert Space' to describe measurements as local entanglement. Ultimately, it aims to clarify that the apparent 'spooky action' in the Schrödinger picture is an artifact of not treating observables as local fields.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Model / system.** A non-relativistic particle undergoing a standard double-slit experiment, involving free evolution phases and interactions with a screen containing two infinitely narrow slits.

**Key observables.** Position and momentum operators defined as functions of both space and time (fields).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Slit separation (d), detection position (s), particle mass (m), and time intervals (t1, t2).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The particle is free during evolution, the slits are infinitely narrow, and the analysis assumes the fundamental validity of locality.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by motivating the need to re-examine non-locality, introduces the Heisenberg picture framework, develops the mathematical derivation of interference using space-time dependent observables, compares projective measurements with the 'Church of the Larger Hilbert Space', and concludes with a discussion on the locality of quantum phases.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a fundamental reinterpretation of quantum measurement and locality, which is highly relevant for those working on the foundations of quantum mechanics and the interpretation of entanglement in open quantum systems.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We present the standard double slit experiment with non-relativistic particles in the Heisenberg Picture of quantum mechanics. Our motivation is threefold. First and foremost, and contrary to some claims in the literature, we show that there is no need to talk about non-locality when explaining the interference fringes. Secondly, we emphasise the fact that even in the non-relativistic regime, and in order to preserve locality, we should define the position and momentum observables of a particle as functions of both space and time (and not just time). Thirdly, our presentation compares the projective measurements in the Heisenberg picture with the "Church of the Larger Hilbert Space", the latter of which is seldom discussed in the Heisenberg picture of quantum mechanics.

</details>

**Main problem.** Surface oxides on the sidewalls of superconducting tantalum devices contribute to dielectric losses, degrading the performance of transmon qubits.

**Main result.** The damascene fabrication process successfully replaces the metal/air interface with a metal/substrate interface, leading to a statistical improvement in quality factor of approximately 2x for pristine interfaces compared to buried oxide interfaces.

**Method.** The researchers used a 300 mm scale damascene process involving trench etching, Ta deposition, and chemical mechanical planarization (CMP), followed by cryogenic characterization and STEM/EDS analysis.

**Summary.** This paper investigates a new fabrication method called the damascene process to improve the quality of superconducting tantalum resonators. By embedding the metal within the substrate, the researchers replaced lossy metal/air sidewall interfaces with metal/substrate interfaces. The study demonstrates that avoiding native oxide formation at these interfaces can significantly increase the resonator's quality factor. This approach is highly scalable, utilizing 300 mm industrial-scale fabrication processes, which is crucial for the future of large-scale quantum computing hardware.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Model / system.** The physical system consists of Tantalum (Ta) based superconducting coplanar waveguide (CPW) resonators fabricated on 300 mm Si (100) wafers, including both capacitive and inductive coupling configurations.

**Key observables.** Resonance frequencies, coupling quality factor (Qc), internal quality factor (Qi), kinetic inductance (Lki), and electric field distribution profiles.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Temperature range of 25 mK to 350 mK, power levels from -40 to -80 dBm, and 300 mm wafer scale.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The numerical simulations neglected kinetic inductance initially, requiring a post-hoc correction to match experimental resonance frequencies.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the damascene fabrication process flow; Figure 2 displays SEM images of the resonator structures; Figure 3 illustrates simulated electric field distributions; Figure 12 shows Qi as a function of temperature; Figure 13 compares quality factors across different device types.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of surface oxides, describes the damascene fabrication process and experimental setup, presents numerical simulations and characterization results (STEM/EDS), discusses the performance of different device variants, and concludes with an analysis of loss mechanisms.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Surface oxides contribute to losses in superconducting transmon devices resulting in degraded performance. We explore the use of the damascene process to replace the sidewall native oxide of a device with a metal/substrate interface. We simulate sidewall oxidation by burying an oxide layer during fabrication. We observe a modest improvement between the two types of devices, which is suggestive of a reduction in the surface participation ratio.

</details>

**Main problem.** The study aims to characterize various spatiotemporal wave modes (spiral, target, stripe, and disordered) and their phase transitions in a non-equilibrium six-state Potts model.

**Main result.** The researchers identified distinct wave modes (SP, TG, ST, and DS) and found that the transition between even- and odd-numbered state dominance is a first-order transition. They also distinguished between forward and backward three-state waves based on their boundary dynamics.

**Method.** Monte Carlo simulations using the Metropolis algorithm were performed on square and hexagonal lattices to observe pattern formation and coarsening dynamics.

**Summary.** This paper investigates the variety of wave patterns that emerge in an active six-state Potts model. By using Monte Carlo simulations, the author identifies spiral, target, stripe, and disordered waves and maps their existence across different interaction energies. The study reveals that transitions between certain wave modes are first-order and explores how these patterns evolve during coarsening. The findings provide a detailed classification of complex spatiotemporal dynamics in non-equilibrium statistical mechanics.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Model / system.** An active six-state Potts model on 2D square and hexagonal lattices, which is a non-equilibrium system where local balance holds but global detailed balance is violated.

**Key observables.** Temporal and spatial correlation functions, oscillation amplitude and period, probability of phase coexistence, cluster size, and the wave ratio order parameter.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Flipping energy (h), non-flip contact energy (Jnf), contact energy for same states (Jkk), and diagonal state interactions.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The system is assumed to follow local balance for flips between neighboring states, and the dynamics are governed by a rock-paper-scissors-like mechanism.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows a phase diagram of modes; Figure 7 shows the dependence of various quantities on contact energy; Figure 12 demonstrates first-order transition behavior via the scaling of susceptibility.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the active Potts model, presents a phase diagram of wave modes, analyzes the quantitative transition between disordered and spiral waves, investigates the emergence of target and stripe waves, details the dynamics of three-state waves, and concludes with a discussion on lattice robustness and transition types.

**Why it may be interesting.** The study of non-equilibrium phase transitions and pattern formation in multi-state systems is highly relevant to understanding driven-dissipative dynamics in open quantum systems and active matter.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Wave propagation can be observed in various nonequilibrium systems. In this study, we investigated the properties of several wave modes in active six-state Potts models using Monte Carlo simulations of square and hexagonal lattices. Disordered and spiral (SP) waves of six states are formed under weak and strong repulsions at nonflip contacts, respectively. The target (TG) and stripe (ST) waves were found to emerge under stronger repulsion. These three wave modes (SP, TG, and ST) can temporally coexist in small systems near the transition points but they do not switch in large systems or far from these transition points. During coarsening from randomly mixed states to ST waves, SP waves appear at an intermediate stage. The SP wave modes of three even- or odd-numbered states (states $s=0,2,4$ or $s=1,3,5$) emerge under two conditions: repulsion at the diagonal contact and attraction at nonflip contacts. Previously thought to be identical for both conditions, the wave types were found to differ, comprising forward and backward waves ($s=1\to 3\to 5\to 1$ or $s=1\to 5\to 3\to 1$), whose domain boundaries move by the two-step and four-step forward flips, respectively. The transition between the waves of the even- and odd-numbered states is first-order for both the forward and backward waves.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.22151"></a>
### [Long-Range Order in Coupled $D$-dimensional Kuramoto Oscillators](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.22151v1)

**Authors:** Zhongpu Qiu, Tianyi Wu, Linkai Zhang, Sheng Fang, Jun Meng, Jingfang Fan, Hugues Chaté  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.22151v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `driven-dissipative phase transition` **3/5** · 🔥 `non-equilibrium universality` **3/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5** · `dissipative systems` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.22151_figures/2604.22151_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. Schematic of the D-dimensional Kuramoto model. Each oscillator ⃗σi evolves under (i) intrinsic rotation Wi⃗σi (generalizing natural frequencies in classical Kuramoto mod- els) and (ii) coupling hij to neighbors ⃗σj ∈Λi. The coupling term hij = K[⃗b −(⃗a ·⃗b)⃗a] aligns ⃗σi with the orthogonal com- ponent of ⃗σj, promoting synchronization (generalizing sine coupling).</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.22151_figures/2604.22151_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> FIG. 2. Phase diagrams for (a) d = 1, (b) d = 2, and (c) d = 3 dimensional coupled D = 3 oscillators with varying system size L. Log-log-log plots of the orientational order parameter ρ versus the inverse system size L−1 at different coupling strengths K for (d) d = 1, (e) d = 2, and (f) d = 3 systems, where colors indicate the values of the order param- eter.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.22151_figures/2604.22151_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> FIG. 3. Spatial patterns and frequency entrainment for cou- pled D = 3 oscillators. (a) d = 1: Finite entrained clusters with functional network structure inserted. (b) d = 2 (c) d = 3: The largest cluster projected on the D = 3 sphere exhibiting hemisphere phase. (d)-(f) Frequency order param- eter r versus coupling strength K for different system sizes L in d = 1, 2, 3 dimensions respectively, where panel (d) uses log scale for the vertical axis.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.22151_figures/2604.22151_fig4.jpg" alt="Figure 4">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> FIG. 4. Order parameters vs coupling strength K for even-D oscillators in d = 2 dimensions. (a,c,e) Orientational order parameter ρ and (b,d,f) frequency order parameter r for D = 4, 6, 8 respectively, with different system length L.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Main problem.** Investigating whether quenched frequency disorder can stabilize long-range order (LRO) in low-dimensional (d=1, 2) vector Kuramoto oscillators, thereby circumventing the Hohenberg-Mermin-Wagner theorem.

**Main result.** Long-range order emerges in low-dimensional lattices only for odd-dimensional oscillators (D), where quenched noise acts as a stabilizing mechanism that maps the system to an effective ferromagnetic model.

**Method.** The authors use two-body dynamics analysis, large-scale numerical simulations, and a real-space renormalization group (RG) framework combined with Bogoliubov-Krylov averaging.

**Summary.** The paper explores the emergence of long-range order in $D$-dimensional Kuramoto oscillators on 1D and 2D lattices. It discovers a parity effect where order only appears when the oscillator dimension $D$ is odd. This happens because odd-$D$ systems possess a null space in their frequency matrices that allows for synchronization, effectively mapping the system to a ferromagnetic model. In contrast, even-$D$ systems remain disordered. The study highlights how quenched noise can serve as a stabilizing force against the fluctuations that usually prevent order in low dimensions.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Model / system.** A D-dimensional vector Kuramoto model (DDKM) on 1D and 2D lattices, featuring alignment coupling and quenched noise introduced via antisymmetric intrinsic frequency matrices.

**Key observables.** Orientational order parameter (rho) and frequency order parameter (r, measuring the fraction of entrained oscillators).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Oscillator dimension (D), lattice dimension (d), coupling strength (K), and the presence of quenched frequency disorder.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the use of antisymmetric matrices for frequency disorder and focuses on the thermodynamic limit via RG.

**Figures summary.** Figure 4 shows the decay of order parameters (rho and r) versus coupling strength (K) for even-D oscillators, demonstrating the absence of LRO.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the model and the problem of LRO in low dimensions, analyzes microscopic two-body dynamics, presents large-scale numerical simulations, performs a real-space renormalization group analysis, and concludes by comparing the results to standard continuous symmetry models.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to many-body dynamics as it demonstrates a novel mechanism where quenched disorder, typically a source of decoherence or disorder, actually stabilizes long-range order in low-dimensional systems.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We show that the long-range order (LRO) strikingly emerges in systems of locally coupled $D$-dimensional vector Kuramoto oscillators on low-dimensional lattices ($d=1,2$), but only for odd $D$. This parity-dependent effect is traced to two-oscillator dynamics, where odd-$D$ units synchronize for any coupling, while even-$D$ pairs require a finite threshold. This fundamental difference selectively seeds collective order in large-scale systems, a phenomenon demonstrated by our numerical simulations. A renormalization group analysis reveals a RG flow to a weak-coupling fixed point for $d \le 2$. In this limit, odd-$D$ systems effectively map to a ferromagnetic model, developing an ordered ``hemisphere" phase, whereas even-$D$ systems remain disordered. Our findings further reveal orientational LRO emerges in both $d=1$ and $d=2$, but frequency LRO requires $d=2$. We contrast these results with the established behavior of models possessing continuous symmetry, highlighting how quenched disorder provides a fundamentally new route to order.

</details>

### [Entanglement Enhanced Sensing with Qubits affected by non-Markovian Dephasing](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.22368v1)

**Main problem.** Investigating whether entanglement, specifically via spin-squeezed states, can enhance the sensitivity of frequency estimation in Ramsey spectroscopy when qubits are subject to non-Markovian, spatially and temporally correlated dephasing noise.

**Main result.** The study finds that while entanglement provides no scaling advantage for noise with a non-zero zero-frequency component (like White or Gaussian noise), it enables scaling beyond the Standard Quantum Limit for noise with a vanishing zero-frequency component (like Linear or Ohmic noise), achieving uncertainties as low as N^{-3/4}.

**Method.** The authors use an analytical framework involving Fourier decomposition of the effective Hamiltonian, the Wiener-Khinchin theorem, and optimization of interrogation time and squeezing parameters to derive scaling laws for various noise spectra.

**Summary.** This paper explores how entanglement can improve the precision of frequency estimation in the presence of complex, non-Markovian noise. It demonstrates that the ability to surpass the Standard Quantum Limit depends critically on whether the noise spectrum has a non-zero component at zero frequency. For specific types of noise, such as Ohmic or Linear noise, the authors show that spin-squeezed states can achieve significantly better scaling of sensing uncertainty with the number of probes than separable states. This provides important theoretical insights for developing quantum sensors that are resilient to correlated environmental noise.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Model / system.** A collection of N two-level systems (qubits) undergoing Ramsey spectroscopy, subject to a classical, pure dephasing noise model that is stationary, translationally invariant, and non-Markovian.

**Key observables.** Estimation uncertainty (variance) of the frequency b, metrological gain (r), and dephasing rate gamma(tau).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Number of probes (N), total interrogation time (T), squeezing parameter (kappa), and noise power spectra (White, Gaussian, Linear, Ohmic).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The noise is treated as a classical stochastic process (valid for high-temperature bosonic baths), the dephasing is pure (no X or Y components), and the analysis assumes the long-time and large-N limits.

**Figures summary.** Figure 4 shows metrological gain as a function of N for various noise combinations; Figure 8 displays scaled uncertainty, metrological gain, and optimal interrogation time for GHZ states across different noise spectra.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of sensing under correlated noise, defines the Hamiltonian and noise models, derives a fundamental sensitivity limit based on SNR, analyzes specific scaling laws for different noise spectra (White, Gaussian, Linear, Ohmic), compares different entangled states (OAT, TAT, GHZ), and concludes with optimization results.

**Why it may be interesting.** This paper is highly relevant for researchers in open quantum systems and quantum metrology because it identifies specific noise-correlation regimes where entanglement-enhanced sensing can bypass the Standard Quantum Limit, providing a roadmap for designing robust quantum sensors.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Entanglement has been proposed as a means to improve the sensitivity of sensing weak signals. While the degree of this quantum advantage is well understood in noiseless settings, the situation is more complex under realistic conditions, where the system is subject to decoherence. In this case, the enhancement depends on the specific noise characteristics. Previous treatments of colored noise typically assume that the noise is uncorrelated between successive experiments. Here, we consider the scenario in which the noise exhibits correlations across multiple shots. We derive a simple fundamental limit to the sensitivity based on the fact that the sensitivity cannot be better than the signal-to-noise ratio seen by the probe. Focusing on Ramsey spectroscopy with probes affected by pure classical dephasing, we show that, for suitable spatial and temporal noise correlations, entangled probes achieve a better scaling of the sensitivity with the number of probes than separable states. This demonstrates that entanglement can provide a substantial improvement for Ramsey spectroscopy subject to correlated noise.

</details>

### [Uncovering long-lived relaxation channel and exciton-phonon coupling in \textrm{Ta\textsubscript{2}NiSe\textsubscript{5}} via non-degenerate pump-probe spectroscopy](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.22218v1)

**Authors:** Poulami Ghosh, Anupama Chauhan, Sidhanta Sahu, Sk Kalimuddin, Mintu Mondal, N. Kamaraju  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.22218v1>

**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `correlated cavity matter` **2/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5**

**Main problem.** The study aims to understand the hierarchy of relaxation pathways and the role of exciton-phonon coupling in the excitonic insulator Ta2NiSe5 (TNSe), specifically investigating the nature of the long-lived relaxation channel.

**Main result.** The researchers discovered an unusually long-lived relaxation channel (280-600 ps) attributed to an exciton-phonon bottleneck and identified two distinct coherent phonon modes, one of which (1.0 THz) is strongly coupled to the excitonic order parameter.

**Method.** Temperature-dependent, non-degenerate optical pump-probe spectroscopy with an extended temporal window up to 500 ps was used to monitor transient reflectivity.

**Summary.** This paper investigates the ultrafast dynamics of the excitonic insulator Ta2NiSe5 using extended-window pump-probe spectroscopy. It reveals a previously hidden, long-lived relaxation channel lasting up to 600 ps, which is caused by a bottleneck between excitons and nonequilibrium phonons. The study also distinguishes between phonon modes that couple to the excitonic order parameter and those that are purely structural. These findings provide a clearer picture of how electronic and lattice degrees of freedom interact during a phase transition.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Model / system.** The physical system is Ta2NiSe5 (TNSe), a layered narrow-gap semiconductor exhibiting a concomitant excitonic-insulator and structural phase transition at Tc approx 327 K. The dynamics are analyzed using a modified Rothwarf-Taylor model and an exciton-phonon bottleneck framework.

**Key observables.** Transient differential reflectivity (delta R/R), relaxation time constants (tau1, tau2), coherent phonon frequencies and amplitudes, and the Fano asymmetry parameter.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Transition temperature Tc approx 327 K, pump wavelength 3.14 eV, probe wavelength 1.57 eV, and relaxation scales ranging from sub-picosecond to 600 ps.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the electronic background can be modeled by a multi-exponential decay and that the phonon dynamics can be described by cubic anharmonic decay or Fano-like interference.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows the schematic of the excitonic insulator transition, resistivity anomalies, and crystal structures. Fig 2 shows temperature-dependent transient reflectivity. Fig 5 shows the temperature dependence of phonon amplitudes, frequencies, and linewidths.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the excitonic insulator phase in TNSe, describes the experimental setup and sample growth, presents the discovery of the slow relaxation channel, analyzes the temperature dependence of electronic and phonon modes, and concludes with the implications for exciton-phonon dynamics.

**Why it may be interesting.** The discovery of a long-lived exciton-phonon bottleneck and the observation of Higgs-like mode coupling to the condensate are highly relevant to many-body dynamics and the study of non-equilibrium phase transitions.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

An excitonic insulator represents a quantum phase in which spontaneous condensation of excitons leads to novel many-body phenomena. Ta$_2$NSi$_5$ (TNSe), a layered narrow-gap semiconductor, has emerged as a model platform to probe these correlated excitonic phases and their underlying dynamics below 327 K. In this work, we investigate the nonequilibrium dynamics of TNSe using temperature-dependent, non-degenerate optical pump-probe spectroscopy with a 3.14 eV pump and a 1.57 eV probe, extending the accessible pump-probe delay window up to 500 ps. In addition to the well-established sub-picosecond relaxation channel ($\sim$ 0.7- 0.9 ps) associated with carrier cooling and recombination, accompanied by exciton reformation, we uncover a much slower recovery process with a decay time of $\sim$ 280-600~ps, significantly longer than previously reported. We attribute this unusually prolonged recovery to enhanced scattering between excitons and nonequilibrium phonons, which delays the re-establishment of equilibrium excitonic correlations. On top of this bi-exponential background, we observe two coherent phonon modes at 1.0 and 2.9 THz with distinctly different coupling behaviors. The 1.0 THz mode exhibits an order-parameter-like temperature dependence, consistent with strong coupling to the excitonic condensate in TNSe. In contrast, the 2.9 THz mode does not exhibit any discernible coupling to the excitonic order parameter, and appears to arise from anharmonic lattice dynamics associated with the structural phase transition. Together, these results elucidate the hierarchy of relaxation pathways in TNSe and highlight the importance of extending the temporal detection window in pump-probe measurements to fully capture long-lived exciton-phonon dynamics.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.22295"></a>
### [Certification of genuine non-Gaussian entanglement](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.22295v1)

**Authors:** Lukáš Lachman, Carlos E. Lopetegui-González, Massimo Frigerio, Mattia Walschaers

**Main problem.** Developing a method to certify 'genuine non-Gaussian entanglement'—entanglement that cannot be produced from separable states using any Gaussian evolution.

**Main result.** The authors establish a fidelity-based certification framework that identifies thresholds for recognizing non-Gaussian entanglement and demonstrates its robustness against transmission loss in Fock and hybrid states.

**Method.** A resource theory approach using fidelity to target states compared against calculated thresholds, utilizing the CMA-ES genetic algorithm for optimizing Gaussian unitaries.

**Summary.** This paper introduces a new method to certify genuine non-Gaussian entanglement in bosonic systems. By comparing the fidelity of an experimental state to a target state against a calculated threshold, the authors can distinguish entanglement that is fundamentally non-Gaussian from that which can be generated via Gaussian operations. The study demonstrates this method on Fock states and hybrid states, providing insights into how much loss these states can tolerate while remaining certifiable. This work bridges the gap between entanglement theory and the practical verification of non-Gaussian quantum resources.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Model / system.** Two-mode bosonic systems, specifically focusing on continuous-variable (CV) and hybrid (DV-CV) encodings, including Fock states, photon-subtracted states, and cat-state superpositions.

**Key observables.** Fidelity (F), stellar rank, and transmission efficiency (eta).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Squeezing parameter (r), superposition angle (theta), cat state spacing (alpha), and photon subtraction mode (phi).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis is primarily constrained to pure 2-mode states and assumes a convex resource theory framework for handling mixed states.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the hierarchy of state spaces and the concept of robustness; Figures 4a-h illustrate fidelity thresholds and loss tolerance as functions of squeezing, subtraction angles, and transmission efficiency.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of non-Gaussian entanglement, defines the resource theory framework, presents the fidelity-based certification method, applies it to various bosonic states (Fock, hybrid, photon-subtracted), analyzes loss tolerance, and provides a mathematical decomposition of Gaussian dynamics.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a practical tool for quantum optical experimentalists to verify the presence of high-level quantum resources (non-Gaussianity) that are essential for fault-tolerant quantum computing and advanced quantum metrology.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Entanglement is a key resource for many quantum applications. Understanding fundamental properties of entangled states is an important step towards their practical exploitation. We characterize entanglement in the context of Gaussian and non-Gaussian processes and identify entangled states that cannot be produced by any Gaussian evolution acting on separable states. This distinction exposes intrinsic limitations of Gaussian operations and highlights the role of non-Gaussian operations as an important resource. We apply this framework to develop a certification method that connects entanglement theory with quantum non-Gaussianity - an advanced quantum feature that is essential for several quantum applications. Our approach tailors the certification to experimentally relevant states that can be produced in current quantum optics experiments. We demonstrate this certification for recognizing the genuine non-Gaussian entanglement of various entangled Fock states and hybrid entangled states.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.21967"></a>
### [Random entanglement percolation on realistic quantum networks](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.21967v1)

**Authors:** Alessandro Romancino  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.21967v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `entanglement & information structure` **3/5** · `dissipative systems` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.21967_figures/2604.21967_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Fig. 1. – Left: probability density of the polarization-dependent loss P. Right: corresponding induced SCP density obtained from Eq. (4). The Mecozzi–Shtaif and Galtarossa–Palmieri curves were matched to the same mean PDL, ⟨P⟩≈2.35 dB, while the Lin–Jiang model used the ﬁve- element setting (0.8, 1.2, 1.4, 1.0, 0.7) dB.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Main problem.** Investigating how heterogeneity in edge entanglement, specifically caused by polarization-dependent loss (PDL), affects entanglement percolation in quantum networks.

**Main result.** The study demonstrates that while Random Classical Entanglement Percolation (RCEP) depends only on the mean singlet-conversion probability, Random Quantum Entanglement Percolation (RQEP) depends on the full distribution of probabilities due to the quantum penalty of the q-swap protocol.

**Method.** The author uses a mathematical mapping between PDL magnitude and singlet-conversion probabilities (SCP) and analyzes the percolation thresholds using RCEP and RQEP frameworks with q-swap preprocessing.

**Summary.** This paper explores how random fluctuations in edge entanglement affect the ability to establish long-range connectivity in quantum networks. By focusing on polarization-dependent loss in photonic networks, the author shows that the distribution of entanglement quality matters significantly for quantum protocols. Specifically, the 'quantum penalty' in RQEP makes the network performance sensitive to the entire probability distribution of the edges, not just the average. This provides a more realistic framework for designing robust quantum communication infrastructures.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Model / system.** A photonic quantum network where qubits are encoded in orthogonal polarization modes and edges are subject to random singlet-conversion probabilities (SCPs) induced by polarization-dependent loss (PDL).

**Key observables.** Singlet-conversion probability (SCP), PDL magnitude (in dB), and the quantum penalty constant (C).

**Important parameters / regimes.** PDL magnitude (P), mean SCP (mu), standard deviation (sigma), and the weak-PDL regime.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study focuses specifically on PDL as a source of randomness and assumes the use of the q-swap preprocessing protocol.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the probability density of PDL for three different models (left) and the resulting induced SCP densities (right).

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by recalling classical and quantum entanglement percolation, introduces the PDL-induced SCP model, derives the mapping between PDL and SCP, analyzes the RCEP and RQEP regimes, and concludes with specific examples of induced distributions from real-world PDL models.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a concrete physical link between realistic fiber-optic impairments (PDL) and the fundamental limits of entanglement distribution in quantum networks.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study random entanglement percolation in heterogeneous quantum networks, where the singlet-conversion probabilities (SCPs) of the edges are drawn from a probability distribution rather than being fixed. After briefly recalling random classical and random quantum entanglement percolation, we focus on polarization-dependent loss (PDL) as a physical source of random edge entanglement in photonic networks. In this setting, polarization imbalance induces a simple map from the PDL magnitude to the edge SCP. We illustrate this map for representative PDL models and discuss the resulting implications for entanglement percolation.

</details>

### [Signature of paraparticles: a minimal Gedankenexperiment](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.22178v1)

<figure class="paper-figure-card">

<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 10.</b> Low-resolution page preview, page 11</figcaption>

**Main problem.** The paper seeks to bridge the gap between the theoretical existence of permutation-group parastatistics and the lack of experimental methods to detect them, specifically addressing whether these particles can be distinguished from ordinary bosons and fermions.

**Main result.** The author provides a minimal Gedankenexperiment that maps the detection of Z2 x Z2-graded colored paraparticles onto a measurable chirality-type parity test, demonstrating that certain parastatistical signatures are physically inequivalent to ordinary statistics.

**Method.** The study uses the mathematical framework of Z2 x Z2-graded color Lie (super)algebras and graded Hopf algebras to construct multi-particle sectors and proposes a 'yes/no' projection measurement approach.

**Summary.** This paper proposes a simplified thought experiment to detect 'colored' paraparticles, which are particles that follow statistics governed by the permutation group rather than just the braid group. By using the mathematical framework of color Lie superalgebras, the author shows that these particles possess unique physical signatures in their multi-particle energy spectra and eigenvectors. The detection method is simplified into a parity test that can be implemented using qudits, specifically ququarts. This work is significant because it challenges the long-held belief that parastatistics are indistinguishable from ordinary bosons and fermions.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Model / system.** A minimal single-particle quantum model with a 4-dimensional Hilbert space and a diagonal Hamiltonian, which is then extended to a 2-particle sector. The proposed experimental platform is the manipulation of qudits, specifically ququarts (d=4).

**Key observables.** Energy eigenvalues, energy level degeneracy, and a chirality-type parity measurement via Hermitian projection operators.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The energy level spacing parameter lambda, the sign parameter epsilon (distinguishing fermions from parafermions), and the Z2 x Z2 grading bits.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The experiment assumes the energy levels are non-degenerate and relies on a calibration test where the first measurement is assumed to represent ordinary statistics.

**Figures summary.** The paper includes an array showing four classes of symmetric bilinear maps and a matrix displaying the structure of 4x4 minimal matrix representations for different graded sectors.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from the mathematical background of Z2 x Z2 grading to a minimal single-particle model, followed by multi-particle quantizations, and concludes with a chirality-based detection test for experimentalists.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a concrete theoretical roadmap for using high-dimensional quantum computing (qudits) to simulate and detect exotic particle statistics that go beyond the standard bosonic and fermionic paradigms.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Paraparticles beyond bosons and fermions can be exchanged via either the braid group (anyons, existing up to $D=2$ space dimensions) or the permutation group; in the latter case the space dimensions are not limited. Besides being predicted, anyons have been experimentally detected. The situation differs for paraparticles exchanged via the permutation group ("permutation-group parastatistics").The first test to detect their theoretical signature was published in 2021 (for $Z_2\times Z_2$-graded parafermions; it was soon followed by a second paper proving the detectability of $Z_2\times Z_2$-graded parabosons). Later on, two further papers proved theoretical signatures of permutation-group parastatistics. These works demonstrate that, in certain situations, a long-held belief on the "conventionality of parastatistics" argument can be evaded: some measurements of permutation-group paraparticles cannot be recovered from ordinary bosons/fermions. The main question now is how to experimentally detect or engineer in the laboratory such paraparticles. For this aim a minimal setup for the theoretical test is here provided: a Gedankenexperiment (a simplified version of the two tests published in 2021) which, essentially, is a flow chart of logical operations. The key point is to present, to experimentalists, the necessary steps to be simulated/realized in the laboratory (possibly, by manipulating qudits). In this minimal setup, the detection/engineering of paraparticles is mapped into a chirality test. The mathematical setting is based on $Z_2\times Z_2$-graded color Lie (super)algebras and derived mathematical structures.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.22194"></a>
### [Qubit-Scalable CVRP via Lagrangian Knapsack Decomposition and Noise-Aware Quantum Execution](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.22194v1)

**Main problem.** The scalability of the Capacitated Vehicle Routing Problem (CVRP) on NISQ devices is limited by the high qubit and gate requirements of direct QUBO encodings and the instability of classical optimization parameters in noisy environments.

**Main result.** The authors developed an end-to-end hybrid pipeline that uses Lagrangian decomposition to reduce qubit requirements and a learned RL-based controller to stabilize multiplier updates, achieving significantly lower optimality gaps on real hardware compared to classical subgradient methods.

**Method.** A hybrid framework combining Lagrangian relaxation to decompose CVRP into bounded-width knapsack subproblems, a reinforcement learning policy (PPO) for multiplier updates, and a constrained contextual bandit for hardware-aware execution.

**Summary.** This paper presents a practical end-to-end framework for solving large-scale vehicle routing problems using near-term quantum computers. By decomposing the complex CVRP into smaller, manageable knapsack subproblems, the authors bypass the qubit-width bottleneck. They further enhance performance by using reinforcement learning to stabilize the optimization loop and a contextual bandit to adaptively select the best quantum hardware configurations. The approach demonstrates that hybrid, decomposition-centric pipelines are more viable for NISQ-era applications than monolithic quantum encodings.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Model / system.** The problem is modeled as a CVRP on a complete graph, transformed via Fisher-Jaikumar linearization and Lagrangian relaxation into 0-1 knapsack subproblems encoded as QUBO/Ising Hamiltonians with a 'tilted' quadratic penalty.

**Key observables.** Optimality gap relative to Best Known Solutions (BKS), routing quality, feasibility rate of quantum samples, and hardware metrics like gate-budget hit rate and latency.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Vehicle capacity, customer demand, travel costs, Lagrangian multipliers (lambda), penalty strength (rho), and headroom parameter (s).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The method assumes that a feasible global route can be reconstructed from stochastic quantum samples using a deterministic repair heuristic and that hardware descriptors are available for the bandit layer.

**Figures summary.** Figures demonstrate the scaling of logical qubits versus problem size, micro-benchmarks comparing different penalty encodings (Tilted vs. Taylor), and the distribution of quantum jobs across heterogeneous backends (IBM, Rigetti, IQM).

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the scalability bottleneck, proposes a decomposition-based framework, details the Lagrangian relaxation and knapsack encoding, describes the RL-based multiplier control and hardware-aware bandit layer, and concludes with experimental validation on simulators and real QPUs.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Hybrid quantum optimization for vehicle routing faces a practical bottleneck: direct QUBO encodings of CVRP quickly exceed near-term qubit and gate budgets, while quantum evaluations are expensive, noise-limited, and sensitive to backend and circuit configuration. We address this gap with an end-to-end decomposition pipeline that converts CVRP into bounded-width quantum subproblems and treats quantum execution as a decision problem within the optimization loop. Starting from a Fisher--Jaikumar assignment linearization, we apply Lagrangian relaxation to dualize customer-assignment couplers, yielding independent per-vehicle knapsack subproblems that admit QUBO/Ising evaluation. To replace brittle subgradient tuning, we learn a multiplier-update controller using expert-guided pretraining followed by reinforcement-learning fine-tuning, with rewards based on execution-realized progress and route reconstruction. We also introduce a constrained contextual bandit as a hardware-aware execution layer that selects backend and circuit configuration with feasibility screening, enabling adaptation across heterogeneous noisy resources and parallel multi-QPU scheduling. Computational results on multiple CVRPLIB families show that the decomposition yields stable bounded-width subproblems across instance sizes, learned multiplier updates improve end-to-end routing quality relative to classical subgradient control under matched budgets, and hardware-mode configuration reduces median optimality gaps relative to static execution choices in our test set. We do not claim quantum advantage. Instead, the contribution is a practical end-to-end framework for scaling hybrid quantum CVRP optimization through OR decomposition, learning-augmented dual control, and adaptive hardware-aware execution.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.22718"></a>
### [Replica Tensor Train](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.22718v1)

**Authors:** Miha Srdinsek, Gabriel Gouraud, Xavier Waintal  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** numerical methods · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.22718v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `entanglement & information structure` **3/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.22718_figures/2604.22718_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Figure 2: Example of the S matrix computed with dual Monte Carlo before (left) and after (right) the orthogonalization procedure. The matrix was constructed when solving the Ising model in Fig. 5. The new states are compressed to χ = 2.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.22718_figures/2604.22718_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> Figure 3: Error on the energy during a replica space optimization by application of (1 −τ ¯H)P for three choices of ¯H, see text. The number of active sub-MPS is one (blue), two (orange) and four (green). The optimization is purely algebraic but the calculation of the energy is performed using VMC sampling. The parameters are hx = 3, L = 20, χ = 2, τ = 0.1 and α = 0.08 for the ¯HHV DD′ curve (green line).</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.22718_figures/2604.22718_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> Figure 4: Error on the energy after a replica space optimization versus system size L. The number of active sub-MPS is one (blue), two (orange) and four (green), see text. The accuracy of the RTT is robust with respect to system size. Same parameters as in Fig.3.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.22718_figures/2604.22718_fig4.jpg" alt="Figure 4">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> Figure 5: Dual Monte Carlo optimization steps starting from the solution of ¯HHV (star). At each step, Eq. (48) is solved. The eigenvalues of the equation (arrows) are compared with the actual energies of the state after the compression (points).</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.22718_figures/2604.22718_fig5.jpg" alt="Figure 5">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 5.</b> Figure 6: Error of χ = 2 RTT versus hx in the paramagnetic region of the phase diagram. The parameters and optimization procedure are the same as in Table 1: (solid line) replica space optimization using ¯HHV, followed by (dashed line) phys- ical space optimization using ¯OZZ,DD′. The error is estimated using the V-score, δE/E ≈0.2Nvar[H]/E2, see Ref. [28]</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Main problem.** The fundamental trade-off between expressivity (capturing volume-law entanglement) and computability (avoiding expensive gradient descent or high-dimensional contractions) in many-body physics.

**Main result.** The development of the Replica Tensor Train (RTT), which allows for the algebraic, non-gradient-based finding of ground states for highly entangled systems, achieving high accuracy in 2D Ising models.

**Method.** A hybrid approach combining Tensor Networks (specifically an extended Tensor Train) with Quantum Monte Carlo (Dual Monte Carlo) to sample observables from a volume-law entangled ansatz.

**Summary.** This paper introduces the Replica Tensor Train (RTT), a new numerical technique that merges the algebraic strengths of tensor networks with the sampling power of Monte Carlo. Unlike standard tensor networks that struggle with 2D entanglement or neural networks that rely on noisy gradient descent, RTT can capture volume-law entanglement and find ground states through purely algebraic methods. The authors demonstrate that this approach can efficiently solve the 2D transverse-field Ising model with high precision. This method bridges the gap between the high expressivity of neural quantum states and the high efficiency of traditional tensor network algorithms.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Model / system.** A 2D transverse-field Ising model on a square lattice, specifically studied in the paramagnetic region near the quantum critical point.

**Key observables.** Ground-state energy per site, energy variance (V-score), and energy error relative to reference Green Function Monte Carlo results.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Bond dimension (chi), number of replicas (K), transverse field strength (hx), and the specific path (sigma) through the physical sites.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The method assumes the ground state can be represented with a relatively small internal bond dimension and requires a predefined path through the physical degrees of freedom.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 compares different tensor network ansätze (MPS, PEPS, RTT); Figure 2 shows the effect of stochastic orthogonalization on the overlap matrix; Figure 3 illustrates energy error reduction via different operator distributions; Figure 4 shows error scaling with system size; Figure 5/6 show optimization convergence and error as a function of the transverse field.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the RTT ansatz and its three equivalent mathematical interpretations, describes the algebraic ground-state search and the Dual Monte Carlo sampling method, demonstrates optimization in both replica and physical space, and validates the approach using the 2D transverse-field Ising model.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a powerful new tool for simulating highly entangled 2D systems by bypassing the optimization bottlenecks (like barren plateaus) of Neural Quantum States while maintaining the expressivity needed for volume-law entanglement.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We describe a numerical many-body technique that is based on both tensor networks and quantum Monte Carlo. The variational ansatz is a tensor network that can harvest volume-law entanglement. It is constructed from a tensor train to which one applies a set of non-local operators that force several indices of the tensor train to represent the same physical index, hence its name -- replica tensor train (RTT). From the tensor network toolbox, it inherits the possibility to make linear combinations of these states and apply a certain class of operators. We can therefore find the ground-state of a local Hamiltonian in a purely algebraic way as in standard tensor network algorithms -- i.e. without using gradient descent methods. On the other hand, the volume-law structure forbids calculating physical observables directly. In much the same way as on a quantum computer where one can prepare a state but can only sample it at the end, here we have to use Markov Chain Monte Carlo to compute the observables. We further show that the approach can be extended to build Krylov-subspace ground-state methods within the variational manifold. We illustrate the different algorithms on a two-dimensional spin model with a transverse magnetic field, which can be solved by this approach at low computational cost.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.22298"></a>
### [A variational formulation of stochastic thermodynamics: Spatially extended systems](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.22298v1)

**Authors:** Héctor Vaquero del Pino, François Gay-Balmaz, Hiroaki Yoshimura, Lock Yue Chew  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.22298v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5** · `dissipative systems` **2/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.22298_figures/2604.22298_page2.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Low-resolution page preview, page 2</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.22298_figures/2604.22298_page3.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> Low-resolution page preview, page 3</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">

<figure class="paper-figure-card">

<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 10.</b> Low-resolution page preview, page 11</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Main problem.** The lack of a systematic, top-down variational method for constructing stochastic field theories that are guaranteed to be thermodynamically consistent and satisfy local detailed balance.

**Main result.** The development of a unified geometric variational framework that extends Hamilton's principle to spatially extended systems, ensuring that the resulting stochastic partial differential equations naturally satisfy the second law of thermodynamics and fluctuation-dissipation relations.

**Method.** A generalized Lagrange-d'Alembert principle and an axiomatic approach where the second law is introduced as a constraint, utilizing functional calculus and path integral formulations.

**Summary.** This paper provides a new mathematical foundation for building stochastic models of continuous, spatially extended systems. By using a variational principle that treats the second law of thermodynamics as a fundamental axiom, the authors ensure that any derived model is automatically thermodynamically consistent. This framework allows for the systematic derivation of complex models, such as those for complex fluids, while guaranteeing that they obey essential physical laws like local detailed balance and fluctuation theorems.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Model / system.** Spatially extended, infinite-dimensional continuous-variable systems (stochastic field theories) described by functional Langevin equations and Fokker-Planck equations in a Hilbert space.

**Key observables.** Entropy production (system, medium, and total), entropy production rate, heat, work, and fluctuation theorems.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Local detailed balance (LDB), fluctuation-dissipation relations (FDR), and the Stratonovich convention for noise.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The use of the Stratonovich convention (white-noise limit), time-scale separation, and the assumption of a large heat bath.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of thermodynamic consistency in field theories, develops a variational framework using an extended phase space, proves the derivation of entropy production and fluctuation theorems, and provides mathematical appendices for closed, multicomponent, and open systems.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Stochastic field theories are often constructed phenomenologically, without a systematic assessment of thermodynamic consistency or local detailed balance. This may hinder a physical description of irreversibility at the field-theoretic level beyond the standard statistical formulation of stochastic thermodynamics. Here, we develop a variational formulation for thermodynamically consistent stochastic field theories by extending Hamilton's principle of classical field theory. Introducing the second law as an axiom yields a consistent local thermodynamic structure in which novel fluctuationdissipation relations emerge naturally, ensuring local detailed balance. Remarkably, the resulting entropy production takes the same form as in standard stochastic thermodynamics, up to a reformulation in an extended phase space incorporating both configurational and thermal variables. This correspondence extends key results, including individual trajectory-level thermodynamics and fluctuation theorems. The construction is formulated within a unified geometric framework based on a generalized Lagrange-d'Alembert principle, providing a top-down connection between phenomenological modeling and thermodynamic consistency. Potential applications include thermodynamically consistent modeling of complex fluids, Lagrangian reduction by symmetry in continuum systems, and structure-preserving numerical schemes for stochastic partial differential equations.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.22232"></a>
### [On the Interplay Between Noise, Bell Violation, and Cascade Error Correction in Device-Independent Quantum Key Distribution](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.22232v1)

**Authors:** Nguyen Duong Hoang Duy, Nguyen Trinh Dong, Vu Tuan Hai, Le Vu Trung Duong, Nguyen Van Tinh  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.22232v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `dissipative systems` **2/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `quantum measurements` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>

Each party uses a Quantum Random Number Generator (QRNG) to choose measurement settings and performs measurements on their respective photons, with outcomes obtained through state-selective ionization and fluorescence readout. The outcomes are then stored locally. The results are communicated over an authenticated channel, where a Bell test verifies the presence of entanglement and detects potential eavesdropping. Afterward, key sifting retains only compatible measurement results, error correction reconciles discrepancies, and privacy amplification (universal...</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.22232_figures/2604.22232_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> Fig. 2: Standard DIQKD. In this work, Cascade error correction is incorporated into the Information reconciliation stage of the (classical) post-processing layer.</figcaption>

<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.22232_figures/2604.22232_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
