</div>

**Main problem.** Investigating how noise impacts the CHSH value and evaluating the effectiveness of the Cascade error correction protocol in Device-Independent Quantum Key Distribution (DIQKD).

**Main result.** Noise significantly degrades Bell nonlocality (CHSH value), but the Cascade protocol effectively reduces the error ratio, with most corrections occurring in the first few rounds. The study concludes that hardware-level noise reduction is more critical for DIQKD viability than increasing error correction complexity.

**Method.** A high-fidelity simulation of an entanglement-swapping-based DIQKD protocol using a depolarizing noise model and the Cascade error correction algorithm (parity checking and binary search).

**Summary.** This paper studies the impact of noise on the security and efficiency of Device-Independent Quantum Key Distribution. By simulating an entanglement-swapping setup with rubidium atoms, the authors show that while noise degrades the necessary Bell inequality violations, the Cascade error correction protocol can significantly reduce bit errors. The findings suggest that improving physical hardware to reduce noise is more important for successful DIQKD than implementing more complex classical error correction algorithms.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Model / system.** A simulated event-ready entanglement swapping scheme between two remote rubidium atoms connected via optical fiber, incorporating a depolarizing noise model in a 2x3 state space.

**Key observables.** CHSH parameter (S), Quantum Bit Error Rate (QBER), and Secret Key Rate.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Critical QBER threshold of 0.082; noise levels ranging from 0% to 100%; measurement settings for Alice and Bob.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Validity of quantum mechanics, trusted classical components, authenticated classical channels, and the requirement for loophole-free Bell tests.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the DIQKD protocol workflow; Figure 2 illustrates the division between quantum and classical layers; Figure 3 plots the CHSH parameter and QBER against noise levels; Figure 4 provides a heatmap of the remaining error ratio.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the DIQKD framework, describes the simulation setup and noise model, details the implementation of the Cascade protocol, presents results on noise impact and error correction efficiency, and concludes with implications for hardware design.

**Why it may be interesting.** It addresses the practical robustness of quantum protocols against environmental decoherence and evaluates the trade-off between physical-layer noise and classical post-processing overhead, which is central to building scalable quantum networks.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Device-Independent Quantum Key Distribution (DIQKD) provides information-theoretic security by relying solely on the violation of Bell inequalities, eliminating the need to trust the quantum devices. However, practical implementations of DIQKD are highly sensitive to noise. Efficient error correction during the classical post-processing stage is important for improving the fidelity. This work investigates the impact of noise on the Clauser-Horne-Shimony-Holt (CHSH) value and evaluates the effectiveness of Cascade error correction. The protocol is applied iteratively to correct errors via parity checking and binary search procedures. Simulation results show that noise significantly degrades the CHSH value, reducing the strength of nonlocal correlations required for secure DIQKD. Nevertheless, Cascade reduces the error ratio, and most corrections occur within the first several rounds. These findings highlight the importance of classical error correction in improving DIQKD systems.

</details>

**Main problem.** Developing a mean-field theory to describe the stability, formation, and phase transitions of various condensed structures (such as traffic jams and immobile bands) in a three-state active lattice gas model.

**Main result.** The mean-field theory successfully predicts the existence of ordered states like immobile bands and traffic jams, though it fails to capture certain complex transitions, such as the emergence of Type-I traffic jams from dense bands, which are observed in Monte Carlo simulations.

**Method.** The authors use a mean-field theory (MFT) approach involving a triangular lattice of coupled nonlinear differential equations integrated via a fourth-order Runge-Kutta scheme, validated against Monte Carlo simulations.

**Summary.** This paper presents a mean-field theoretical framework to study the emergence of complex patterns in an active matter model. It investigates how different types of dense structures, like traffic jams and immobile bands, evolve under varying noise and density. While the theory captures the stability of many ordered phases, the authors highlight discrepancies with simulations, particularly regarding certain types of traffic jam formations. This work provides insight into the non-equilibrium phase transitions of self-propelled particle systems.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Model / system.** A three-state active lattice gas model (3-SALGM) on a triangular lattice where self-propelled particles have three possible velocity orientations, subject to local alignment, excluded-volume interactions, and noise.

**Key observables.** Orientational order parameter (Phi), density profiles, orientation fractions, and a deviation parameter (tilde sigma) to quantify deviations from disorder.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Global density (rho_g), noise intensity (eta), and alignment strength (g).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The mean-field theory assumes that the occupancy probabilities of neighboring sites are mutually independent to factorize configuration probabilities.

**Figures summary.** Schematics of the lattice model and neighborhood; initial configurations for various structures (IB, MB, TJ); density and orientation profiles; and phase diagrams/order parameter plots showing transitions vs. noise.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the 3-state model, develops the mean-field equations, presents numerical integration results, compares MFT predictions with Monte Carlo simulations for various initial configurations, and discusses the limitations of the MFT approach.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We develop a mean-field description including spatial structure for a simplified version of the three-state active matter model studied by Venzel et al. (Phys. Rev. E 110, 014109 (2024)). The resulting triangular lattice of coupled nonlinear differential equations are integrated numerically using a fourth-order Runge-Kutta scheme. Starting from various ordered initial configurations, we probe the stability of the corresponding stationary states, revealing the presence of various high-density ordered structures in the density(\r{ho})-noise(η) plane. The results are compared with Monte Carlo simulations of the simplified model, yielding, in certain cases, unexpected transitions between ordered configuration types.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.22627"></a>
### [The Exact Replica Threshold for Nonlinear Moments of Quantum States](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.22627v1)

**Authors:** Shuai Zeng  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.22627v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `quantum measurements` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.22627_figures/2604.22627_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. Exact replica threshold for nonlinear moments. The staircase ⌈t/2⌉indicates the exact threshold for the pure mo- ments tr(ρt) established here, together with its extension to the observable-weighted family tr(Oρt) considered in this work.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Main problem.** Determining whether the number of available quantum state replicas marks a sharp information-theoretic boundary for the estimation of nonlinear observables like tr(rho^t).

**Main result.** The paper proves that the exact replica threshold for fixed-order pure moments is ceil(t/2), where having fewer replicas leads to a dimension-growing sample complexity barrier.

**Method.** The authors use a reduction to a spectrum-testing promise problem, constructing 'hard-pair' probability distributions using Newton's identities and applying Haar-integrated ensemble analysis and permutation inequalities.

**Summary.** This paper identifies a sharp, discrete resource boundary in quantum state estimation. It proves that for estimating nonlinear moments of order t, having ceil(t/2) replicas allows for polynomial-sample estimation, whereas having one fewer replica forces the required sample size to grow with the system dimension. This result establishes that the number of coherent replicas is a fundamental, qualitative resource for nonlinear quantum tasks.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Model / system.** A sample/copy-access model with replica-limited joint measurements applied to unknown d-dimensional quantum states.

**Key observables.** Pure nonlinear moments tr(rho^t) and observable-weighted moments tr(O*rho^t).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Replica number s, moment order t, Hilbert space dimension d, and number of measurement rounds T.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The results focus on the replica-limited model and assume fixed-order moments as the dimension d goes to infinity.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the staircase-like nature of the exact replica threshold as a function of the moment order t.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of replica thresholds, establishes the lower bound via a reduction to spectrum testing and hard-pair construction, proves the upper bound using existing hybrid frameworks, and extends the threshold law to a broad family of weighted observables.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Joint measurements on multiple copies of a quantum state provide access to nonlinear observables such as $\operatorname{tr}(ρ^t)$, but whether replica number marks a sharp information-theoretic resource boundary has remained unclear. For every fixed order $t\ge 3$, existing protocols show that $\lceil t/2\rceil$ replicas already suffice for polynomial-sample estimation of $\operatorname{tr}(ρ^t)$, yet it has remained open whether one fewer replica must necessarily incur a sample-complexity barrier growing with the dimension. We prove that this is indeed the case in the sample/copy-access model with replica-limited joint measurements: any protocol restricted to $\lceil t/2\rceil-1$ replicas requires dimension-growing sample complexity, while $\lceil t/2\rceil$ replicas suffice by prior work. Thus the exact replica threshold for fixed-order pure moments is $\lceil t/2\rceil$. Equivalently, for fixed-order pure moments, one additional coherent replica is not merely useful but marks the exact threshold between polynomial-sample estimation and a dimension-growing regime in the replica-limited model. We further show that the same threshold law extends to a broad family of observable-weighted moments $\operatorname{tr}(Oρ^t)$, including Pauli observables and other observables with bounded operator norm and macroscopic trace norm. Coherent replica number therefore acts as a genuinely discrete resource for nonlinear quantum-state estimation.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.22029"></a>
### [Apparent Planckian scattering from local polaron formation](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.22029v1)

**Authors:** Brian Yong-Ho Lee, Chaitanya Murthy  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.22029v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `correlated cavity matter` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>

<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.22029_figures/2604.22029_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">

Each trace consists of 50000 steps after an initial 5000 burn steps that are not shown. Quantitative diagnostics for each chain are shown in Table I.</figcaption>

<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.22029_figures/2604.22029_fig4.jpg" alt="Figure 4">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> FIG. 4. Scattering rates extracted by all methods and for all spatial directions (when relevant) for the two-dimensional square lattice with model parameters L = 20, t = 1, t′ = −0.3t, ν = 1.4. Quasiparticle and transport scattering rates are extracted from the quenched and annealed averaged spectral function and optical conductivity, respectively. All methods produce approximately T-linear scattering rates. The x- and y-direction extractions of the Drude and Drude weight (DW) methods agree with each other as expected since quenched disorder averaging restores the symmetry of the lattice.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.22029_figures/2604.22029_fig5.jpg" alt="Figure 5">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 5.</b> FIG. 5. Quenched and annealed averaged spectral function evaluated near the nodal (a–c) and antinodal (d–f) k-points at representative values of the coupling and temperature for the two-dimensional square lattice with model parameters L = 20, t = 1, t′ = −0.3t, ν = 1.4.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.22029_figures/2604.22029_fig6.jpg" alt="Figure 6">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 6.</b> FIG. 6. Quenched and annealed averaged optical conductivity in the x-direction (a–c) and y-direction (d–f) at representative values of the coupling and temperature for the two-dimensional square lattice with model parameters L = 20, t = 1, t′ = −0.3t, ν = 1.4. The x- and y- direction optical conductivities agree with each other as is expected since quenched disorder averaging restores the symmetry of the lattice.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.22029_figures/2604.22029_fig7.jpg" alt="Figure 7">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 7.</b> FIG. 7. Density of states (a–c) and localization length of single particle eigenstates (d–f) at representative values of the coupling and temperature as a function of energy measured with respect to the chemical potential for the two-dimensional square lattice with model parameters L = 20, t = 1, t′ = −0.3t, ν = 1.4. The density of states is smoothed with a Gaussian kernel with standard deviation σ = 0.25. There remains a finite density of extended states at and near the chemical potential, indicating that our results are not driven by Anderson localization.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Main problem.** Explaining the origin of 'apparent Planckian scattering' (T-linear scattering rates with a universal slope) without relying on complex many-body effects or fine-tuning.

**Main result.** The authors show that T-linear scattering emerges from local polaron formation in a disordered Holstein model, where the scattering rate slope saturates to a constant value as coupling increases.

**Method.** Classical Monte Carlo simulations combined with Exact Diagonalization and a Local Approximation to treat the disordered electron-phonon coupling.

**Summary.** This paper proposes that the observed T-linear 'Planckian' scattering in certain metals can arise from the formation of local polarons in the presence of disordered electron-phonon coupling. Using Monte Carlo simulations of the Holstein model, the authors demonstrate that as coupling increases, the scattering rate slope saturates to a constant value. They show that this regime is characterized by a violation of Matthiessen's rule, where changes in coupling affect the intercept rather than the slope. The mechanism is local and does not depend on Anderson localization.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Model / system.** A 2D Holstein model in the adiabatic limit featuring spatially disordered electron-phonon coupling, where the coupling strength is drawn from a random distribution.

**Key observables.** Transport scattering rate (Gamma_tr), quasiparticle scattering rate (Gamma_qp), longitudinal optical conductivity, Drude weight, and Binder cumulant.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Adiabatic limit (omega_0 -> 0), dimensionless local coupling (lambda), temperature (T), and electron filling (nu).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study is restricted to the adiabatic limit; the local approximation assumes the joint distribution of phonon displacements is a product of local distributions.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows T-linear scattering rates and the saturation of the slope alpha alongside Binder cumulants; Figure 2 displays distributions of effective site potentials across different coupling regimes; Figure 3 shows Monte Carlo convergence traces.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the Planckian scattering problem, describes the disordered Holstein model, presents Monte Carlo/ED results demonstrating the emergence of T-linear scattering, analyzes the saturation of the slope alpha via polaron formation, and discusses the violation of Matthiessen's rule.

**Why it may be interesting.** The mechanism provides a way to generate universal-like T-linear transport signatures from local, disordered interactions rather than requiring global many-body entanglement or fine-tuned critical points.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We propose a simple mechanism for apparent Planckian scattering based on local polaron formation, in which $Γ_\text{tr} = Γ_0 + αk_BT / \hbar$ with $α\sim O(1)$ emerges from quasielastic scattering without fine tuning. We provide evidence for our proposal in Monte Carlo simulations of the Holstein model with disordered electron-phonon coupling in the adiabatic limit. Our mechanism generates a finite interval of couplings in which the slope $α$ is approximately constant, coinciding with the onset of local polaron formation. In this regime, Matthiessen's rule is dramatically violated (or obeyed, depending on one's point of view) in that changes to the couplings, which in perturbation theory would alter the slope $α$, instead change the intercept $Γ_0$. We conjecture that a version of our mechanism applies to any system with a dominant disordered interaction that can drive polaron formation. This potentially includes regimes of the recently introduced disordered-Yukawa-coupling strange metal models where polaron formation is not suppressed.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.22187"></a>
### [Dynamically Corrected Bethe-Salpeter Equation Solver for Self-consistent $GW$ Reference on the Matsubara Frequency Axis](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.22187v1)

**Authors:** Ming Wen, Gaurav Harsha, Dominika Zgid  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** numerical methods · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.22187v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.22187_figures/2604.22187_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Figure 1. In counterclockwise order, the workflow of BSE@scGW proceeds as follows: (a) It begins with a mean-field calculation (HF in this study). (b) The density-fitted integrals and resulting matrices are then passed into the scGW cycle and iterated until self-consistency is reached. (c) The scGW quasiparticle energy levels are extracted using the QP approximation. (d) These QP energies, together with the corresponding output matrices, are subsequently used in the static BSE Casida equation. (e) The resulting static BSE solution is then dynamically corrected through plasmon-pole fitting. The shorthand “a.u.” stands for atomic unit. (f) In the final step, the dynamically corrected...</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.22187_figures/2604.22187_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> Figure 2. Charged excitations of N2 calculated at the scGW/aug-cc-pVTZ level of theory. The negative and posi- tive half-axes contain the ionization potential (IP) peaks and electronic affinity (EA) peaks respectively. The QP energy levels serve as the initial inputs for the subsequent BSE cal- culation shown in FIG. 1 (e) and (f).</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">

<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> Figure 3. Feynman diagram of the particle-hole Bethe– Salpeter equation (see Eqn. (16)). Subscripts signify valence and conduction orbitals. BSE kernel Ξ is approximated as the sum of the electron-hole exchange term U and the electron- hole attraction term −W in Eqn. (27).</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">

</figure>

<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.22187_figures/2604.22187_fig7.jpg" alt="Figure 7">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 7.</b> Fig. 6 shows how three singlet excitation energies ob- tained with BSE@scGW converge for the two different basis set families. The corresponding numerical values are provided in Table S1 of the Supplementary Material. For the cc-pVXZ series, since the electron-hole energy differences enter as inputs in the BSE Hamiltonian, this instability in QP energy levels propagates directly into the excitation energies, leading to the poor convergence observed in Fig. 6. By contrast, aug-cc-pVXZ basis sets better represent both the spatially extended virtual or- bitals and the associated screening effects in W. It leads to more stable QP gaps, and hence excitation energies converge well with respect to...</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Main problem.** The need for a computationally efficient and accurate method to calculate neutral excitation energies (optical/core excitations) while overcoming the starting-point dependence and static approximation limitations of standard GW-BSE approaches.

**Main result.** The development of a dynamically corrected BSE@scGW solver that achieves accuracy comparable to high-level wavefunction methods (CCSD, CC3) for small and medium-sized molecules by incorporating frequency-dependent screening.

**Method.** A self-consistent GW (scGW) reference evaluated on the Matsubara frequency axis, combined with a dynamical correction to the static Casida formulation using a plasmon-pole model.

**Summary.** This paper introduces a new solver for the Bethe-Salpeter Equation (BSE) that uses a self-consistent GW reference to eliminate starting-point bias. By implementing a dynamical correction via a plasmon-pole model, the authors incorporate frequency-dependent screening effects into the static Casida equation. The method is benchmarked against high-level quantum chemistry methods and shows high accuracy for singlet and triplet excitation energies in small molecules. This approach provides a robust and efficient way to study neutral excitations in molecular systems.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Model / system.** The study focuses on molecular systems, using small molecules like H2, N2, and H2O as benchmarks, within the framework of many-body perturbation theory (Hedin's equations) and the Green's function formalism.

**Key observables.** Singlet and triplet excitation energies, quasiparticle energies (IP, EA, fundamental gap), and excitation spectra.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Matsubara frequency grids (fermionic and bosonic), inverse temperature (beta), and the plasmon-pole parameters (pole location and strength).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Neglect of the vertex function (Gamma approx delta), use of the quasiparticle approximation, and the adiabatic approximation (neglecting off-diagonal elements of the eigenvector matrix).

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the BSE@scGW workflow; Figure 2 compares quasiparticle energy extraction methods; Figure 3 illustrates the BSE kernel Feynman diagram; Figure 4 shows H2 excitation energies vs bond length; Figure 5 and 6 demonstrate basis set convergence for water.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the scientific problem of BSE limitations, details the BSE@scGW workflow and mathematical framework on the Matsubara axis, describes the implementation of the dynamical correction via the plasmon-pole model, presents benchmarking results against FCI and CCSD for various molecules, and discusses convergence and limitations.

**Why it may be interesting.** The paper presents an advanced numerical scheme for treating frequency-dependent many-body interactions and dynamical screening, which is highly relevant to the development of accurate many-body dynamics and spectroscopic simulations.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We present a Bethe-Salpeter equation (BSE) solver based on a self-consistent $GW$ reference evaluated on the Matsubara frequency axis, referred to as BSE@sc$GW$. The self-consistent $GW$ starting point provides a robust quasiparticle description and reduces sensitivity to the initial mean-field reference compared to one-shot $GW$-based approaches. We further introduce a dynamical correction to the static Casida formulation via a plasmon-pole model. This scheme incorporates simple dynamical screening effects while retaining the efficiency of an effective eigenvalue problem. The resulting dynamically corrected BSE@sc$GW$ yields excitation energies in close agreement with high-level wavefunction-based benchmarks for both singlet and triplet excitations of small molecules. Overall, the accuracy of the dynamic BSE@sc$GW$ approach arises from the combination of a well-converged single-particle reference and the inclusion of frequency-dependent screening effects.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.22448"></a>
### [Electrostatic-Elastic Softening and Ultraviolet Instability Driven by Non-DLVO Interactions in Charged Colloidal Crystals](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.22448v1)

**Authors:** Hao Wu, Zhong-Can Ou-Yang  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.22448v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.22448_figures/2604.22448_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. Effective screening wave vector squared as a function of the coupling parameter. The normalized squared screening wave vector κ2 eff/κ2 0 = 1/(1 −ξ) diverges at the critical coupling ξ = 1. For ξ &lt; 1 (blue curve), κ2 eff &gt; 0, corresponding to standard exponential decay of correlations. For ξ &gt; 1 (red curve), κ2 eff &lt; 0, signaling that the effective screening wave vector becomes purely imaginary. Formally, in the minimal continuum model, this signals the dominance of electrostatic attraction over elastic restoring forces; in real colloidal crystals, the discrete lattice cutoff and higher-order elastic terms regulate this ultraviolet divergence.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.22448_figures/2604.22448_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> Figure 2 summarizes the stability regions in the (ξ, q) plane. For ξ &lt; 1, the uniform phase is stable (light red region). At ξ = 1, the short-wavelength modulus b vanishes, and the system becomes marginally stable at q →∞. For ξ &gt; 1, the uniform phase is unstable for all wave vectors q &gt; qc (light blue region). In the minimal continuum model, this unstable region extends to q →∞; however, in a real crystal the discrete lattice imposes a maximum wave vector qmax, confining the physical insta- bility to a finite shell qc &lt; q &lt; qmax (light orange shad- ing). The long-wavelength modes q &lt; qc (light green re- gion) remain linearly stable even for ξ &gt; 1, reflecting the perfect ionic screening...</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.22448_figures/2604.22448_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> FIG. 2. Stability diagram of the uniform phase in the minimal electrostatic-elastic coupling model. The light red region (ξ &lt; 1) corresponds to the stable uniform phase. The vertical gray dashed line marks the critical cou- pling ξ = 1, where the short-wavelength elastic modulus van- ishes. For ξ &gt; 1, the purple solid curve shows qc = κ0/√ξ −1, above which the uniform phase becomes unstable. The light blue region (q &gt; qc) indicates the continuum prediction of in- stability extending to arbitrarily short wavelengths. In a real colloidal crystal, the discrete lattice provides an ultraviolet cutoff qmax ∼π/a; the physically relevant unstable band is the light orange shaded region qc &lt; q &lt;...</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Main problem.** Investigating the mechanical stability and the nature of the ultraviolet instability in charged colloidal crystals caused by the coupling between electrostatic and elastic degrees of freedom.

**Main result.** The study finds that while the macroscopic bulk modulus remains stable due to perfect ionic screening, the short-wavelength elastic modulus softens and vanishes at a critical coupling strength, leading to a localized structural instability.

**Method.** The authors use a Gaussian fluctuation analysis on a continuum field theory, integrating out electrostatic fluctuations to derive an effective wave-vector-dependent elastic modulus.

**Summary.** This paper explores how the interaction between electric fields and mechanical strain affects the stability of colloidal crystals. It demonstrates that while the crystal remains stable at large scales, a strong enough coupling can cause the material to fail at very short length scales. This 'ultraviolet instability' is physically limited by the discrete nature of the crystal lattice. The work provides a clearer understanding of how non-DLVO interactions can trigger local structural collapse in charged soft matter.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Model / system.** A 3D continuum model of a charged colloidal crystal (an ordered array of large particles) permeated by a mobile monovalent ion fluid, coupling Poisson-Boltzmann electrostatics with Hookean elasticity via a local dilation term.

**Key observables.** Effective elastic modulus Gamma(q), effective screening wave-vector kappa_eff, and the dimensionless coupling parameter xi.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The coupling parameter xi (critical at xi=1), the wave-vector q, the lattice cutoff q_max, and the screening wave-vector kappa_0.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The model uses a continuum approximation for both the lattice and the ions, assumes an isotropic elastic medium, and neglects higher-order gradient terms which leads to unphysical UV divergence in the limit q to infinity.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the transition of the effective screening wave-vector as a function of the coupling strength; Figure 2 provides a stability diagram in the (xi, q) plane mapping stable, unstable, and unphysical regimes.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the coupling problem, defines the Hamiltonian and continuum model, performs a Gaussian fluctuation analysis to derive the effective modulus, analyzes stability regimes (long vs. short wavelength), and concludes by discussing the physical regulation of the instability by the lattice cutoff.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Colloidal crystals permeated by mobile ions exhibit a coupling between electrostatic and elastic degrees of freedom that renormalizes the effective screening length and induces wave-vector-dependent elastic softening. Building on a recently proposed continuum model [\textit{Commun. Theor. Phys.} \textbf{77}, 055602 (2025)], we perform a rigorous Gaussian fluctuation analysis to elucidate the stability limits of the homogeneous phase. By integrating out the electrostatic fluctuations, we derive the effective elastic modulus $Γ(q)$ as a function of wave vector $q$. We show that the long-wavelength modulus $Γ(0)$ remains identically equal to the bare modulus $βK$, protected by perfect ionic screening. In contrast, the short-wavelength modulus $Γ(q\to\infty) = βK(1-ξ)$ softens as the electrostatic-elastic coupling $ξ\equiv 2βn_0 v_0^2 K$ increases, vanishing at a critical value $ξ=1$. For $ξ>1$, the fluctuation spectrum exhibits a negative eigenvalue for all wave vectors $q > q_c = κ_0/\sqrt{ξ-1}$, signaling an ultraviolet instability of the uniform phase. In a real colloidal crystal, this divergence is regulated by the discrete lattice cutoff $q_{\max}\simπ/a$, confining the physical instability to a finite band $q_c < q < q_{\max}$. The macroscopic limit $q\to 0$ remains unconditionally stable for all $ξ$. The transition at $ξ=1$ thus marks the onset of short-wavelength mechanical failure, while macroscopic elastic stiffness remains intact. Our analysis clarifies the proper physical interpretation of the minimal coupling model and provides a consistent picture of how non-DLVO interactions can drive local structural collapse in charged colloidal crystals.

</details>

### [Magnetoelastic Waves in Ferromagnetic Thin Films Mediated by Dipolar Interactions](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.22523v1)

**Authors:** Hiroki Yoshida, Ryohei Kono, Manato Fujimoto, Motoki Asano, Daiki Hatanaka, Kei Yamamoto, Shuichi Murakami  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.22523v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `spintronics-quantum-optics interface` **2/5**

A ferromagnetic thin film with thickness L in the z direction and infinite extent in the x and y directions is con- sidered. Magnetic moments precess around an axis parallel to the in-plane external magnetic field H0, which is applied at an angle φ with respect to the x axis. The external magnetic field is applied (a) parallel to the propagation direction, H0 ∥k (φ = 0), (b) at φ = π/4, and (c) perpendicular to the propagation direction, H0 ⊥k (φ = π/2). Insets show magnified views of representative band crossings. In (a), φ = 0, anticrossings appear between BVW and Lamb modes only when they have the same C2x eigenvalue (same color). When the C2x symmetry of the...</figcaption>

**Main problem.** The lack of a unified theoretical framework to describe magnetoelastic coupling in ferromagnetic thin films specifically mediated by long-range magnetic dipolar interactions.

**Main result.** The study predicts the hybridization of magnetostatic waves and Lamb waves in YIG thin films, identifying anti-crossings with hybridization gaps ranging from 0.1 to several MHz.

**Method.** A Lagrangian-based approach incorporating Maxwell's equations and elastic deformations, solved using the Finite Element Method (FEM) and a Green-function approach.

**Summary.** This paper investigates how magnetic dipolar interactions contribute to the coupling between magnetic and elastic waves in ferromagnetic thin films. By incorporating elastic deformations into the derivation of dipolar fields, the authors show that magnetostatic waves and Lamb waves can hybridize. Using YIG as a model material, they demonstrate the appearance of frequency gaps at anti-crossings. The study also highlights how the symmetry of the applied magnetic field dictates the strength and presence of this coupling.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Model / system.** An infinitely extended, isotropic ferromagnetic thin film (specifically Yttrium Iron Garnet) of thickness L under an in-plane external magnetic field.

**Key observables.** Dispersion relations (frequency vs. wavenumber), hybridization gaps, and anti-crossings.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Film thickness L = 500 nm, external field mu0H0 = 0.1 T, and YIG material parameters (density, Lamé constants, gyromagnetic ratio, and saturation magnetization).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Magnetostatic approximation (neglecting retardation effects), linearized dynamics (small deviations of magnetization and displacement), and the quasi-static condition.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows a schematic of the system and the mechanism of deformation-induced dipolar modification; Figure 2 displays dispersion curves and anti-crossings for different magnetic field orientations (phi = 0, pi/4, pi/2).

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of dipolar-mediated coupling, develops a mathematical framework using Lagrangian and Eulerian coordinate transformations, derives the effective field and force density, and concludes with numerical FEM simulations of YIG films.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Magnetoelastic coupling mediated by magnetic dipolar interactions is theoretically investigated in ferromagnetic thin films under an in-plane magnetic field. We develop a theoretical description that incorporates dipolar fields derived from Maxwell's equations in the presence of elastic deformations. The resulting coupled equations of motion predict hybridization between magnetostatic and Lamb waves. Numerical calculations for a yttrium iron garnet (YIG) film reveal anti-crossings in the dispersion relations, with hybridization gaps ranging from $0.1$ to several MHz.

</details>

### [QAssemble: A Pure Python Package for Quantum Many-Body Theory](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.22223v1)

<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> Fig. 3 Self-consistent workflows for Hartree–Fock and GW approximations. (a) The HF loop iterates between H0, V, SigH, SigF, and Hamiltonian classes to solve H = H0 + ΣH + ΣF self- consistently for the density matrix n. (b) The GW loop extends (a) by additionally computing the irreducible polarizability P = GG (P), the dynamically screened interaction W = V + V PW (W), and the correlation self-energy ΣGW = −GW C (SigGWC), with G combining all contributions via the Dyson equation. Boxes indicate classes; arrows denote data flow between components.</figcaption>

**Main problem.** The difficulty of implementing complex, many-body algorithms (like GW and Hartree-Fock) due to the lack of user-friendly, transparent, and extensible software frameworks that can handle electron correlations in materials.

**Main result.** The development of QAssemble, a pure-Python package that achieves up to a 60x speedup over traditional loop-based Matsubara implementations by using systematic vectorization and the Discrete Lehmann Representation.

**Method.** An object-oriented, pure-Python approach leveraging NumPy, SciPy, and libdlr, utilizing systematic vectorization of kernels and the Discrete Lehmann Representation (DLR) for frequency-axis compression.

**Summary.** The authors present QAssemble, a new pure-Python package designed for many-body electronic structure calculations. By replacing traditional Python loops with vectorized array operations and using the Discrete Lehmann Representation, the package achieves significant speedups for GW and Hartree-Fock calculations. The design emphasizes 'hackability' and transparency, making it an ideal tool for researchers prototyping new many-body diagrams. It successfully captures the physics of graphene and provides a scalable foundation for future extensions like DMFT.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Model / system.** The framework is applied to correlated quantum materials, specifically validated using graphene (two-orbital model) and a five-orbital extended Hund-Hubbard model with local and non-local Coulomb interactions.

**Key observables.** One-particle Green's functions, self-energy, polarizability, screened Coulomb interaction, density matrix, and spectral functions.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Lattice/orbital structures, Matsubara frequency, imaginary time, temperature, and Coulomb interaction strengths (U, V, J).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The current implementation is limited to Tight-Binding, Hartree-Fock, and GW approximations; it prioritizes code transparency and extensibility over maximum raw performance.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the software architecture and workflow; Figure 2 displays the class hierarchy; Figure 4 compares graphene band structures, density of states, and spectral functions across TB, HF, and GW levels.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the scientific challenge of many-body software, describes the QAssemble object-oriented architecture, details the numerical strategies (vectorization and DLR), validates the physics using graphene, and benchmarks performance using a multi-orbital Hubbard model.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

QAssemble is a pure-Python package for the quantum many-body problem. It implements various functional approaches, such as tight-binding, Hartree-Fock, and GW approximations within a unified object-oriented architecture. Each physical concept--crystal structure, Hamiltonian, Green's function, self-energy, polarizability, screened Coulomb interaction--is represented as a distinct class. The modular design prioritizes code clarity and extensibility, leveraging NumPy, SciPy, and libdlr for numerical operations. Performance-critical kernels, including the polarizability bubble, Dyson equation inversion, and lattice Fourier transforms, are systematically vectorized and combined with the discrete Lehmann representation to achieve practical efficiency within a pure-Python environment. We validate QAssemble on the electronic structure of graphene with local and non-local interactions. Furthermore, benchmarks on a five-orbital extended Hund-Hubbard model demonstrate that this strategy delivers up to a 60x speedup over traditional loop-based Matsubara implementations. QAssemble supports both batch execution for production calculations and interactive workflows for method development.

</details>

<img src="2604.22611_figures/2604.22611_fig7.jpg" alt="Figure 7">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 7.</b> Figure 6: Theoretical predictions for the fractions ϕss, ϕrs versus “fitness graph” epistasis 1 −γ∗for the</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.22611_figures/2604.22611_fig8.jpg" alt="Figure 8">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 8.</b> Figure 7: Statistical analysis of the computational RNA landscape reconstructed by ViennaRNA for the</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates the relative frequency and evolutionary impact of simple sign epistasis (SSE) versus reciprocal sign epistasis (RSE) in fitness landscapes.

**Main result.** The authors prove that SSE is the necessary and sufficient condition for evolutionary detours (paths involving back-mutations) and show that SSE is much more frequent than RSE in weakly epistatic, experimentally resolved landscapes.

**Method.** The study combines analytical derivations using Gaussian integration and mean-field approximations with large-scale simulations and statistical analysis of empirical landscapes (e.g., MAGELLAN, ViennaRNA).

**Summary.** This paper explores how different types of genetic interactions, known as epistasis, shape the paths evolution takes through genotype space. It identifies that 'simple sign epistasis' is a key driver of evolutionary detours, where organisms must temporarily move through less-fit states to reach higher peaks. While most landscapes are dominated by this type of interaction, the authors also identify specific structured models where reciprocal interactions can dominate. The findings bridge theoretical landscape models with real-world biological observations.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Model / system.** The paper analyzes various fitness landscape models, including House of Cards (HoC), Rough Mount Fuji (RMF), NK models, and spin-glass-like pairwise allelic incompatibility models.

**Key observables.** Fractions of epistatic motifs (phi_m, phi_ss, phi_rs), correlation of fitness effects (gamma), and accessibility measures (direct vs. indirect paths).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Interaction regimes (dense vs. sparse), scaled slope (s/sigma), and interaction degree (I).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Many analytical derivations assume Gaussian-distributed fitness effects and permutation invariance (unstructured landscapes).

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 visualizes ME, SSE, and RSE motifs and their fitness graphs; Figure 2 compares theoretical predictions to simulations; Figure 4 compares empirical RSE/SSE fractions; Figure 7 analyzes the RNA secondary structure landscape.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the types of epistasis, provides mathematical proofs for the link between SSE and detours, derives analytical predictions for various landscape models, compares these predictions to empirical biological data, and discusses exceptions in structured/sparse models.

**Why it may be interesting.** The use of spin-glass-like models (Ising/SK models) and the study of rugged landscapes with epistatic interactions are directly relevant to understanding energy landscapes and optimization in disordered many-body systems.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

In epistatic fitness landscapes, the fitness effect of a mutation depends on the genetic background and may even switch between deleterious and beneficial depending on the presence of another mutation. Epistatic interactions may cause both mutations to change the sign of each other's fitness effects (reciprocal sign epistasis) or only one mutation to do so (simple sign epistasis). Both these forms of epistasis influence evolutionary trajectories. While reciprocal sign epistasis has been associated with multi-peaked landscapes and their ruggedness, the role and relative frequency of simple sign epistasis in fitness landscapes have not been systematically investigated. Here, we prove that the presence of simple sign epistasis is associated with evolutionary detours, i.e., indirect, longer fitness-increasing paths to fitness peaks that include back-mutations. We also show that in experimentally resolved, weakly epistatic landscapes, simple sign epistasis occurs much more frequently than reciprocal sign epistasis. This result is consistent with the theoretical predictions we derive for most landscape models, with the exception of the block model and of landscapes dominated by pairwise allelic incompatibilities, such as RNA stability landscapes. Our results suggest that detours represent a general feature of evolutionary trajectories in weakly epistatic landscapes.

</details>

### [Anisotropy of spin waves in the field-polarized phase of Fe-doped MnSi](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.21985v1)

**Authors:** I. N. Khoroshiy, A. Podlesnyak, D. Menzel, M. C. Rahn, D. S. Inosov, A. S. Sukhanov, S. E. Nikitin  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.21985v1>

**Main problem.** The study aims to investigate the unexpected and pronounced anisotropy of spin-wave stiffness in the field-polarized phase of the cubic chiral magnet Fe-doped MnSi, which contradicts standard theoretical models.

**Main result.** The researchers discovered that spin-wave stiffness is highly anisotropic, with values significantly higher parallel to the applied field (14.7 meV A^2) than perpendicular to it (7.6 meV A^2), suggesting a need to revise current theoretical understandings of cubic chiral magnets.

**Method.** High-resolution inelastic and elastic neutron scattering measurements were performed using the Cold-neutron Chopper Spectrometer (CNCS) at the Spallation Neutron Source.

**Summary.** This paper reports the discovery of significant spin-wave stiffness anisotropy in the field-polarized phase of Fe-doped MnSi. While standard models for this cubic material predict isotropic spin-wave dispersion, experimental neutron scattering reveals that stiffness is nearly twice as large parallel to the magnetic field as it is perpendicular to it. The authors suggest that this unexpected behavior might be driven by the magnetic field inducing a symmetry reduction in the electronic Fermi surface. This finding challenges existing theoretical frameworks for chiral magnets and necessitates a re-evaluation of how magnetic interactions are modeled in these systems.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Model / system.** The physical system is Fe-doped MnSi (Mn0.9Fe0.1Si), a B20-type itinerant chiral magnetic material, studied in its field-polarized ferromagnetic state.

**Key observables.** Spin-wave dispersion, spin-wave stiffness (A), magnetic propagation vector (km), energy gap (E), and magnetic reflection intensity.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Magnetic field (B), temperature (T ~ 1.8-2 K), Fe concentration (x), and critical field (Bc).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The authors rule out single-ion anisotropy, anisotropic exchange, and dipole-dipole interactions as the primary causes, instead speculating on symmetry reduction of the Fermi surface.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows magnetic phase diagrams; Fig 2 displays magnetic reflection intensity and neutron scattering slices under different cooling protocols; Fig 3 shows the magnetic field dependence of the propagation vector and reflection intensity; Fig 6 compares extracted spin-wave stiffness values for different directions.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of skyrmion-stabilizing interactions, describes the experimental setup and sample preparation, presents the neutron scattering results regarding spin-wave dispersion and anisotropy, evaluates potential physical mechanisms for the observed anisotropy, and concludes with implications for theory.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Chiral magnetic textures, such as skyrmions, are of great interest to the condensed matter community due to their novel transport properties. The stabilization of topologically non-trivial magnetic phases, like the skyrmion lattice in MnSi, is governed by underlying magnetic interactions which can be probed via measurements of spin-wave excitations. Here, we report high-resolution inelastic neutron scattering (INS) measurements of the spin waves in Fe-doped Mn$_{0.9}$Fe$_{0.1}$Si deep within its field-polarized ferromagnetic state. We observe non-reciprocal spin waves with a parabolic dispersion that shifts linearly with magnetic field. Crucially, the spin-wave stiffness is highly anisotropic, with values of 14.7 meV $\rm{\mathring{A}}$$^2$ parallel to the applied field and 7.6 meV $\rm{\mathring{A}}$$^2$ perpendicular to it. This pronounced anisotropy in a cubic material is inconsistent with standard theoretical models for MnSi and indicates a necessity to revise our theoretical understanding.

</details>

### [Beyond Variational Bias: Resolving Intertwined Orders in the Hubbard Model](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.21978v1)

**Main problem.** Investigating how architectural bias in different variational ansätze leads to conflicting physical conclusions regarding competing orders in the Hubbard model.

**Main result.** By using symmetry restoration and variance reduction, three different neural-network ansätze (determinant, particle-hole, and Pfaffian) converge to a consistent physical picture of coexisting superconducting and stripe orders.

**Method.** Variational Monte Carlo using Transformer-based backflow fermionic wave functions, combined with symmetry projection (translational and rotational) and zero-variance extrapolation.

**Summary.** This paper addresses a critical issue in computational many-body physics: the tendency of different variational wave function architectures to favor different physical phases. By studying the 2D Hubbard model, the authors show that while different neural-network ansätze initially predict conflicting stripe or superconducting orders, applying symmetry restoration forces them to converge to a single, shared physical state. This demonstrates that energy minimization alone is insufficient to identify the ground state in systems with competing orders and highlights the necessity of tracking correlation functions during optimization.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Model / system.** The two-dimensional t-t' Hubbard model on an 8x8 square lattice with periodic boundary conditions, specifically focusing on the regime with U=8, t'=-0.2, and 1/8 hole doping.

**Key observables.** d-wave superconducting order parameter (Delta_SC), stripe spin order parameter (M^2_str), spin structure factor S(k), and energy variance.

**Important parameters / regimes.** U = 8.0, t' = -0.2, hole doping delta = 1/8, 8x8 lattice size.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study assumes that the true ground state can be recovered through systematic variance reduction and symmetry restoration, effectively overcoming the initial mean-field bias of the chosen ansatz.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the optimization landscape and convergence of different schemes; Figure 2 plots order parameters against energy variance; Figure 4 compares real-space charge/spin patterns; Figure 7 shows energy vs. variance for extrapolation; Figure 8 and 9 show the impact of symmetry restoration on pairing and spin structure factors.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of variational bias, describes the Transformer-based backflow architecture and the three antisymmetrization schemes, presents results showing how unprojected states diverge, demonstrates convergence through symmetry restoration, and concludes with the physical implications for intertwined orders.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The two-dimensional Hubbard model at finite doping hosts competing or intertwined orders, resulting in conflicting conclusions from different computational approaches regarding its ground state. We show that a key source of such discrepancies is the bias encoded in the variational ansatz. We consider three different Transformer backflow fermionic wave functions based on a Slater determinant, its particle-hole counterpart, and a Pfaffian, initialized without any mean-field pretraining. We show that, despite achieving nearly degenerate, state-of-the-art variational energies, each ansatz converges to a state with qualitatively different spin, charge, and pairing correlations. Upon improving accuracy via symmetry restoration and variance reduction, however, all three converge to the same physical picture: coexisting superconducting and stripe orders. These results demonstrate that variational energy alone is insufficient to identify the ground state in the presence of competing phases, and highlight the importance of tracking how correlation functions evolve as the wave function is systematically improved before drawing physical conclusions.

</details>

The monolithic (dotted) baseline is compared with the distributed MA (solid) and SS-2 (dashed) sched- ules. As EGR increases, the LER of the distributed schemes decreases, with SS-2 con- sistently outperforming MA. Both axes have logarithmic spacing, and EGR is swept over {1, 2, 5, 10, 20, 50, 100, 150, 200} MHz.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Main problem.** Optimizing the scheduling of stabilizer measurements in distributed quantum error correction to balance the trade-off between remote-operation noise (due to waiting for entanglement) and syndrome staleness.

**Main result.** The proposed Skip-Seam-tau (SS-tau) and Adaptive Skip-tau (AST) policies reduce the logical error rate compared to the standard Measure-All baseline, particularly in low-to-moderate entanglement generation rate regimes.

**Method.** The authors develop a scheduling module for syndrome extraction and evaluate it using Stim-based circuit-level noise simulations with a concatenated MWPM decoder.

**Summary.** This paper addresses the performance bottleneck in distributed quantum computing caused by the slow and noisy nature of remote entanglement-based gates. By introducing a scheduling strategy that skips certain boundary (seam) measurements, the authors reduce the accumulation of idling noise on data qubits. They demonstrate that an adaptive approach, which adjusts measurement frequency based on the entanglement generation rate, can significantly improve the logical error rate and approach the performance of monolithic architectures.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Model / system.** A modular quantum architecture consisting of multiple QPUs connected via photonic interconnects, utilizing triangular color codes partitioned across four modules.

**Key observables.** Logical Error Rate (LER)

**Important parameters / regimes.** Entanglement Generation Rate (EGR), code distance (d), physical error rate (p), and the seam-refresh interval (tau).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The decoder and underlying code structure remain unchanged; the study focuses on measurement frequency rather than the classical computational overhead of the scheduler.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the triangular color code structure, the remote CNOT circuit, the performance gap between monolithic and distributed architectures, and plots of LER vs. physical error rate and EGR for various scheduling policies.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of distributed QEC overhead, describes the modular architecture and noise model, presents the proposed SS-tau and AST scheduling policies, evaluates them via Stim simulations, and analyzes performance across different EGR regimes and code distances.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Future quantum architectures are expected to be modular, with quantum processors connecting multiple quantum processing units (QPUs) via photonic interconnects. In topological quantum error correction, such as color codes, this creates seam boundaries where parity checks require remote CNOT operations using heralded Bell pairs. These non-local checks are slower and noisier than bulk local checks because entanglement generation is probabilistic, causing data qubits to accumulate idle noise while waiting for remote operations. A natural way to reduce this overhead is to skip some seam measurements; however, doing so makes seam syndrome information stale and can degrade decoding. The central scheduling problem is therefore to determine how frequently seam checks should be measured so as to balance remote-operation and waiting noise against syndrome staleness.   To address this trade-off, we develop a scheduling module that integrates directly into standard syndrome-extraction circuits. We consider two policies: Skip-Seam-$τ$ (SS-$τ$), which measures all bulk checks every round while measuring seam checks once every $τ$ rounds and copying the most recent syndrome in skipped rounds, and Adaptive Skip-$τ$ (AST), which selects $τ$ as a function of code distance and entanglement generation rate (EGR). We evaluate these policies on triangular color codes under circuit-level noise in Stim, including idling errors induced by Bell-pair generation delays. Our simulations show that SS-tau and AST reduce remote-operation overhead and can lower the logical error rate (LER) relative to the Measure-All (MA) baseline. For physical error rate $p = 10^{-3}$, we identify an EGR regime in which both SS-$τ$ and AST exhibit behavior consistent with fault-tolerant scaling, with LER decreasing as code distance increases. Across these regimes, SS-$τ$ and AST outperform MA.

</details>

**Main problem.** Evaluating the practical performance and constant factor overhead of optimal Quantum Linear System Algorithms (QLSAs), specifically comparing the 'Shortcut' method against Quantum Walk-based approaches.

**Main result.** The Shortcut method is significantly more efficient for non-Hermitian matrices when the solution norm is known, whereas the Quantum Walk method performs better in the unknown-norm regime.

**Method.** Numerical analysis and comparative benchmarking of the Shortcut method (using Kernel Reflection and Kernel Projection via QSVT) against Adiabatic and Randomized Quantum Walk methods using random linear systems.

**Summary.** This paper provides a practical performance analysis of state-of-the-art quantum algorithms for solving linear systems. It compares the recently developed 'Shortcut' method with established Quantum Walk-based solvers. The authors find that while the Shortcut method excels when the solution norm is known, the Quantum Walk method remains more robust and efficient when the norm is unknown. This work is crucial for understanding the real-world utility of these algorithms beyond their theoretical asymptotic complexity.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Model / system.** The study focuses on the Quantum Linear System Problem (QLSP) for square, sparse matrices $A$, including both Hermitian (Positive Definite) and non-Hermitian families.

**Key observables.** Computational cost (number of block-encoding calls), success probability, and solution error (Bures distance/infidelity).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Condition number ($\kappa$), target error ($\epsilon$), solution norm ($\|x\|$), and matrix dimension ($N$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumes access to a block-encoding $U_A$ of the matrix $A$ and that the matrix is normalized such that $\|A\|=1$.

**Figures summary.** Circuit diagrams for the Kernel Reflection algorithm and plots showing the scaling of total cost and error as a function of condition number and error tolerance.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of constant factor analysis, presents the mathematical framework for the Shortcut and Quantum Walk methods, details the numerical simulation setup, and provides comparative results across different matrix regimes and norm-knowledge scenarios.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Optimal quantum linear equation solvers provide complexity $O(κ\log(1/ε))$, where $κ$ is the condition number and $ε$ is the allowable error. The optimal solver using a discrete adiabatic approach [PRX Quantum \textbf{3}, 040303 (2022)] has large analytically proven constant factors for the upper bound on the complexity. The constant factors were later found to be about 1,200 times smaller in numerical testing [Quantum \textbf{9}, 1887 (2025)]. This meant it is about an order of magnitude more efficient than using a randomised approach from [PRX Quantum \textbf{6}, 040373 (2025)], which has far smaller analytically proven constant factors. Recently, a ``Shortcut'' method has been found to provide an optimal solver which also has small proven constant factors. In the present work, we conduct a comprehensive numerical analysis comparing this method with the adiabatic solver for two families of random linear systems. We find that, in the case where the solution norm is \emph{unknown}, the adiabatic solver provides slightly better performance. If the solution norm is \emph{known}, then the shortcut method provides significantly better performance for non-Hermitian matrices.

</details>

**Main problem.** The challenge of realizing robust, fault-tolerant Majorana bound states (MBSs) without the experimental difficulties of 1D nanowires or the need for complex engineered nanostructures and vortices.

**Main result.** The discovery that 2D semi-Dirac materials can host corner Majorana modes through proximity-induced superconductivity, where the system effectively acts as coupled Kitaev chains.

**Method.** A theoretical framework using the Bogoliubov-de Gennes (BdG) formalism, including numerical diagonalization of a tight-binding model and projection onto edge-state subspaces.

**Summary.** This paper proposes a new theoretical platform for realizing Majorana bound states using 2D semi-Dirac materials. By applying Rashba spin-orbit coupling and a Zeeman field to these materials under s-wave superconductivity, the authors show that corner Majorana modes emerge at the edges of finite strips. The system can be effectively described as coupled Kitaev chains. Crucially, the study demonstrates that these corner states are remarkably robust against significant levels of Anderson disorder.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Model / system.** A 2D semi-Dirac system with anisotropic bulk dispersion (quadratic in kx, linear in ky) subject to Rashba spin-orbit coupling, a perpendicular Zeeman field, and s-wave superconducting proximity effect.

**Key observables.** Edge-state dispersion, Majorana polarization, Majorana zero modes at corners, Local Density of States (LDOS), and energy splitting between modes.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Mass term (M0), Rashba coupling (alpha), Zeeman field (BZ), chemical potential (mu), and Anderson disorder strength (w).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The large-width limit (W >> lambda^-1) allows treating opposite edges as decoupled; the superconducting pairing is momentum-independent; and orbital spinors of opposite edges are orthogonal.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows numerical edge-state spectra and spin/orbital components; Figure 2 compares full BdG results to a reduced decoupled model; Figure 3 displays energy spectra for semi-infinite and finite systems; Figure 6 illustrates the stability of the integrated LDOS under increasing disorder.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of Majorana realization, defines the semi-Dirac Hamiltonian and its edge-state properties, derives an effective 1D Kitaev chain model via projection, analyzes the emergence of corner modes in finite geometries, and evaluates the robustness of these modes against Anderson disorder.

**Why it may be interesting.** It proposes a new, naturally occurring 2D platform for Majorana modes that avoids the complexities of engineered nanostructures, which is highly relevant for developing topological qubits for fault-tolerant quantum computing.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Proximity-induced superconductivity in low-dimensional systems offers a powerful pathway to engineer topological superconducting phases in, otherwise, non-superconducting systems. These exotic phases are of fundamental and technological interest due to the presence of robust zero-energy modes, the Majorana bound states. In this work, we propose a theoretical framework to realize Majorana bound states from the edge states of a two-dimensional semi-Dirac system. This anisotropic system, under specific conditions, can host non-chiral edge states that propagate only along particular edges, effectively forming separated one-dimensional channels. We show that the interplay between Rashba spin-orbit coupling and a Zeeman field on this setup provides the right conditions to get an effective p-wave pairing between the edge states by proximity with a s-wave superconductor. In finite geometries, each edge can independently undergo a topological phase transition into a one-dimensional topological superconductor and give rise to four zero-energy modes localized at the strip corners. At low energies, the edge states subspace admits a description in terms of coupled Kitaev chains, providing a clear picture of the origin, robustness, and tunability of the corner Majorana modes. Our results establish semi-Dirac materials as a natural platform for realizing Majorana modes in two dimensions without relying on engineered nanostructures, vortices, or crystalline higher-order topology.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Dynamic Moiré Potentials and Robust Wigner Crystallization in Large-Scale Twisted Transition Metal Dichalcogenides](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.22343v1)

**Authors:** Yifan Ke, Chuanjing Zeng, Xinming Qin, Wei-Lin Tu, Wei Hu, Jinglong Yang  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.22343v1>

**Main problem.** Bridging the gap between theoretical predictions and experimental observations in large-scale moire systems by accounting for how lattice dynamics and thermal fluctuations influence electronic localization and Wigner crystallization.

**Main result.** The inclusion of dynamical effects from lattice vibrations deepens moire potential wells and narrows the conduction band, leading to robust Wigner crystallization and the emergence of a kagome-patterned three-electron state.

**Method.** A machine-learning-based workflow integrating DeePMD for molecular dynamics and DeepH for machine-learned electronic Hamiltonians, combined with DFT-level accuracy (HONPAS) and DMRG simulations.

**Summary.** This paper presents a new machine-learning workflow to simulate large-scale moire supercells that are too large for traditional DFT. By incorporating lattice vibrations and structural relaxation, the authors show that the moire potential wells deepen, which facilitates the formation of robust Wigner crystals. They specifically identify a kagome-patterned electron state at certain doping levels. This approach provides a practical way to connect atomic-scale dynamics to large-scale emergent electronic phenomena in 2D materials.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Model / system.** Twisted bilayer tungsten disulfide (TBWS2) at small twist angles (e.g., 2.28 and 7.34 degrees), modeled using an effective Hubbard model with site-dependent potentials and Coulomb interactions.

**Key observables.** Interlayer distance (d_int), conduction band bandwidth, Local Density of States (LDOS), and electron density distributions.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Twist angles (2.28 to 13.17 degrees), Hubbard U (up to 50 eV), intersite Coulomb interaction V, and temperature (100 K).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The use of a 11-orbital tight-binding model and the assumption that machine-learned potentials accurately capture the essential physics of the large-scale MD trajectories.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the workflow and structural motifs; Figure 2 illustrates interlayer distance evolution; Figure 4 shows conduction bandwidth; Supplemental figures compare DFT packages (VASP vs HONPAS) and band structures.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces a machine-learning workflow, validates its accuracy against plane-wave DFT, applies it to simulate large-scale MD for twisted TMDs, analyzes the resulting electronic structure and band narrowing, and concludes with many-body DMRG simulations of Wigner crystal formation.

**Why it may be interesting.** The paper demonstrates how structural fluctuations and lattice dynamics can be used to tune many-body phases and emergent geometries (like kagome patterns) in a controlled, theoretically predictable way.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Understanding the dynamical evolution of large-scale moiré systems is crucial for connecting theoretical predictions with experimental observations. Here we develop a machine-learning-based workflow, integrating DeePMD and DeepH frameworks with first-principles calculations, to efficiently investigate time-dependent structural and electronic responses in twisted bilayer transition metal dichalcogenides (TMDs) with experimentally relevant moiré supercells containing over 3000 atoms. Using $\mathrm{WS_2}$ as a representative system, we show that low-temperature lattice vibrations and relaxation deepen the moiré potential wells, narrow the lowest conduction band, and facilitate the formation of strongly localized electronic states. Based on DFT-derived moiré potentials that incorporate these dynamical effects, density-matrix-renormalization-group (DMRG) simulations reveal robust Wigner crystallization and a kagomé-patterned three-electron state, consistent with recent experimental observations. Our workflow provides a practical route for exploring large moiré supercells beyond static configurations and offers new insight into the interplay between lattice dynamics, electronic localization, and emergent correlated states in twisted two-dimensional materials.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.22628"></a>
### [Exact relations between the density-density correlators of states in a spin multiplet](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.22628v1)

**Authors:** Ritajit Kundu, Ajit C. Balram  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.22628v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.22628_figures/2604.22628_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. Coulomb energies in units of e2/(εℓ) in the thermodynamic limit of many fully polarized, i.e., with maximal total spin, fractional quantum Hall states as a function of layer separation, d/ℓ, and polarization, γ, which labels the z−component of the spin. Here, ℓis the magnetic length and ε is the dielectric constant of the background host material.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Main problem.** Computing density-density correlation functions and energies for all members of a spin multiplet is computationally expensive because each state must typically be evaluated separately.

**Main result.** The authors derive exact identities that allow the pair-correlation functions and static structure factors of any state in a spin multiplet to be computed using only the properties of the highest-weight (fully polarized) state.

**Method.** The authors use SU(2) spin rotation symmetry to derive analytical relations between correlation functions and apply these to compute thermodynamic energies via integration of the structure factors.

**Summary.** This paper provides a new analytical method to significantly reduce the computational complexity of studying spin-degenerate many-body systems. By deriving exact relations between states in a spin multiplet, the authors show that one only needs to calculate the correlation functions for a single fully polarized state to know the properties of the entire multiplet. They demonstrate the utility of this method by analytically computing the energy of the Halperin-(1,1,1) state and numerically evaluating various other fractional quantum Hall states. This approach is particularly useful for studying bilayer and moiré systems where spin or pseudospin degrees of freedom are present.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Model / system.** The study focuses on many-body systems with spin or pseudospin (layer, orbital, or valley) degrees of freedom, specifically fractional quantum Hall (FQH) states in bilayer or multi-component systems.

**Key observables.** Pair-correlation function g(r), static structure factor S(q), and ground-state Coulomb energy E0.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Layer separation (d/l), polarization/density imbalance (gamma), and total spin quantum number (S).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The relations are limited to states within the same spin multiplet and rely on the assumption of SU(2) spin-rotation symmetry; energy calculations rely on approximate functional forms for g(r).

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows density plots of Coulomb energies for various FQH states (such as Halperin-(1,1,1) and Moore-Read) as a function of layer separation and polarization.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the computational problem, derives exact analytical identities for correlation functions using SU(2) symmetry, applies these relations to calculate energies for specific FQH states, and discusses results for bilayer systems.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We present exact identities relating the pair-correlation functions and static structure factors of states in a spin multiplet. This allows us to compute these density-density correlation functions of all members of the multiplet using just these correlation functions of the highest-weight state. We apply these relations to obtain energies for many fractional quantum Hall (FQH) states. In particular, we analytically compute the energies of the Halperin-$(1,1,1)$ state as a function of density imbalance and layer separation, and numerically evaluate these energies for many other FQH states.

</details>

### [Field-driven phases in a three-dimensional twisted Kitaev model for CoNb$_2$O$_6$: Interplay of frustration and spin-orbit coupling](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.21973v1)

**Authors:** Tom Drechsler, Matthias Vojta
