**Main problem.** The study aims to understand the complex 3D magnetic phase diagram of CoNb2O6, specifically how the interplay of frustrated inter-chain coupling and spin-orbit coupling drives various field-induced phases.

**Main result.** The authors identify six distinct field-driven phases (including commensurate, incommensurate, and spin-flip phases) and demonstrate that the phase diagram is extremely sensitive to even minute changes in the applied magnetic field direction.

**Method.** Semiclassical techniques and linear spin-wave theory are used at zero temperature to map the phase diagram and compute magnetic excitation spectra.

**Summary.** This paper investigates the magnetic properties of the material CoNb2O6 using a 3D twisted Kitaev model. It explores how the competition between frustrated inter-chain interactions and spin-orbit coupling leads to a complex variety of magnetic phases. The study reveals that the phase sequence is highly sensitive to the angle of the applied magnetic field. The theoretical results, including magnetization and excitation spectra, are shown to be in good agreement with existing experimental observations.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Model / system.** A 3D twisted Kitaev model for the magnetic insulator CoNb2O6, featuring Co2+ ions in zigzag chains with bond-dependent Ising interactions, frustrated inter-chain Heisenberg coupling, and an anisotropic g-tensor.

**Key observables.** Longitudinal and transverse magnetization, magnetic excitation spectra (magnon dispersion), dynamical structure factor, and magnetic Bragg peaks.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Inter-chain coupling strength (J_H,l), lattice distortion parameter (alpha), magnetic field magnitude and direction (theta, phi), and Kitaev coupling (K).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The system is treated as an effective spin-1/2 model; dipole-dipole interactions are neglected; semiclassical spin-wave theory requires a manual rescaling of transverse field components to correct for the Ising limit.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the crystal structure; Figure 2 illustrates the twisted Kitaev chain; Figure 3 displays model ground states and spin structure factors; Figure 4 shows phase diagrams; Figures 8-10 present magnetization and dynamical structure factor results.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by defining the 3D twisted Kitaev model and its Hamiltonian, proceeds to describe the semiclassical and spin-wave methods, presents the mapping of the field-driven phase diagram, compares results to experimental data, and concludes with discussions on phase sensitivity and fluctuations.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The Ising chain in a transverse field stands out as a paradigmatic example for a quantum phase transition. CoNb$_2$O$_6$ has been discussed as a material realization of this physics, but it was later realized that its magnetic exchange couplings are more complicated, taking the form of twisted Kitaev chains. Here we study a three-dimensional model for CoNb$_2$O$_6$, taking into account both Kitaev physics and frustrated inter-chain coupling, in applied magnetic field. Using semiclassical techniques at zero temperature, we map out the sequence of field-driven phases for arbitrary field direction; these include phases with commensurate and incommensurate inter-chain order. As a result of spin-orbit coupling, the phase diagram is extremely sensitive to small changes in the field angle. We compute static observables as well as magnetic excitation spectra in the various phases and connect our results to existing experimental data.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.22537"></a>
### [Fusion of light nuclei in a multicluster realization of the three-body problem](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.22537v1)

**Authors:** Mikhail Egorov  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.22537v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">

Rutherford scattering (magenta dash-dotted line) is compared with contributions from the screened Coulomb potential VR and the unscreened Coulomb potential VC (in the limit R →∞). Experimental data for the ground state of 6Li are from [40].</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Main problem.** Determining total cross sections for fusion and breakup reactions of light nuclei while accurately accounting for long-range Coulomb interactions and electron anti-screening effects.

**Main result.** The multicluster approach provides total cross sections for various helium and lithium fusion reactions that are in good agreement with experimental data, specifically demonstrating the importance of the cluster mechanism and Coulomb effects.

**Method.** The work employs Faddeev integral equations in momentum space using a two-potential method to handle the Coulomb t-matrix and a cluster representation for the colliding nuclei.

**Summary.** This paper presents a theoretical framework using Faddeev integral equations to calculate fusion and breakup cross sections for light nuclei. By using a multicluster model, the author accounts for the internal structure of the colliding nuclei and the complex long-range Coulomb interactions. The results show strong agreement with existing experimental data for various helium and lithium reactions. The study also investigates the impact of atomic electron anti-screening on these nuclear processes.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Model / system.** A multicluster realization of the three-body problem involving light nuclei such as 3He, T, D, 4He, and 7Li, where nuclei are treated as bound clusters of nucleons.

**Key observables.** Total cross sections, astrophysical S-factors, breakup amplitudes, and Coulomb t-matrix off-shell effects.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Kinetic energy range T in [1 keV, 20 MeV], Coulomb screening radius, and Sommerfeld parameter.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The calculation uses a truncated iteration of the Faddeev equations (up to n=2) and assumes specific cluster structures for the projectile and target nuclei.

**Figures summary.** The notes mention a Figure 4, but a full summary of all figures is not available from the provided text.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from the theoretical necessity of Faddeev equations to the implementation of the multicluster model, followed by the treatment of Coulomb effects and the presentation of calculated cross sections compared to experimental data.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

This work describes a few-body dynamics method based on the Faddeev integral equations in momentum space for determining the total cross sections of fusion and breakup reactions with two- and three-body final channels in the continuum, employing a cluster representation of the colliding nuclei. Total cross sections were obtained for the reactions $^3\text{He}(T,D)^4\text{He}$, $^3\text{He}(T,np)^4\text{He}$, $^3\text{He}(T,nD)^3\text{He}$, $^3\text{He}(^3\text{He},2p)^4\text{He}$, $^3\text{He}(^3\text{He},pD)^3\text{He}$, $^7\text{Li}(^3\text{He},\phantom{0}^4\text{He})^6\text{Li}$, $^7\text{Li}(^3\text{He},D^4\text{He})^4\text{He}$, and $^7\text{Li}(^3\text{He},T^3\text{He})^4\text{He}$, in which both the projectile and the target nucleus were treated in a cluster representation. The work also implements a two-potential method to determine the Coulomb $t-$matrix and to account for Coulomb effects in short-range dynamics in momentum space. Calculations of the initial-state Coulomb interaction were performed; furthermore, an estimate was obtained for the magnitude of the off-shell effect of the Coulomb $t-$matrix, as well as the magnitude of the atomic electron anti-screening effect on the Coulomb interaction of the colliding nuclei. The calculated contribution of the cluster mechanism to the total cross section of the considered fusion reactions in the kinetic energy range $T\in[1~\text{keV},20~\text{MeV}]$ is in good agreement with known experimental data.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.22447"></a>
### [Long-Range Correlated Random Matrices](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.22447v1)

**Authors:** Abbas Ali Saberi, Roderich Moessner  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.22447v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.22447_figures/2604.22447_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. Numerical simulations of the variance of eigenvalues as a function of L for H = 1 8, 1</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.22447_figures/2604.22447_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> FIG. 2. Numerical simulations of the excess kurtosis (EK) as a function of L for H = 1 8, 1</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.22447_figures/2604.22447_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> FIG. 3. Probability density function P(λ) of scaled eigenvalues for H = 1</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Main problem.** Investigating how long-range algebraic correlations between matrix elements affect the eigenvalue statistics and spectral density of random matrices.

**Main result.** The study identifies a critical threshold at H = 3/4 where the spectral density transitions from heavy-tailed generalized t-distributions to Gaussian statistics, and a rigidity threshold at H = 1/2.

**Method.** Analytical scaling analysis of eigenvalue moments and Wick-type decomposition of the trace of M^4, supported by extensive numerical simulations.

**Summary.** This paper explores how power-law correlations in random matrix elements alter their spectral properties. It identifies a phase transition at a critical exponent H = 3/4, where the eigenvalue distribution shifts from heavy-tailed distributions to Gaussian statistics. The authors also uncover a secondary threshold at H = 1/2 that affects spectral rigidity. These findings provide a new framework for understanding universality in non-invariant, spatially embedded random matrix ensembles.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Model / system.** A symmetric random matrix ensemble where elements are generated via a thresholded Gaussian random field on a 2D square lattice with power-law correlations decaying as r^-2H.

**Key observables.** Spectral density P(lambda), eigenvalue variance, excess kurtosis, and the scaled average of the maximum eigenvalue.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The correlation exponent H, which defines different regimes: 0 <= H <= 1/4 (divergent kurtosis), 1/4 < H < 3/4 (finite positive kurtosis), H = 3/4 (Gaussian transition), and H > 3/4 (standard RMT/semicircle law).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The underlying Gaussian field is zero-mean, and the nonlinear thresholding process preserves the long-range correlation structure of the original field.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows eigenvalue variance scaling with L; Figure 2 shows excess kurtosis scaling with L; Figure 3 shows the probability density function P(lambda) demonstrating data collapse and agreement with analytical predictions.

**Paper structure.** The paper moves from the construction of the correlated matrix model to the derivation of scaling laws for moments, identifies specific critical regimes for the exponent H, and concludes with numerical verification of the spectral transitions.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Motivated by the importance ascribed to correlations in random matrices used to model phenomena in various scientific disciplines, we report how algebraic correlations between matrix elements affect the eigenvalue statistics and spectral density of random matrices. These correlations, introduced through a long-range correlated percolation model, decay as a power law $\propto r^{-2H}$, with exponent $H > 0$. As $H$ varies, both the eigenvalue distribution and excess kurtosis undergo qualitative changes. At the threshold $H_c = 3/4$, characterized by emergent Gaussian statistics, a sign change in excess kurtosis marks a transition from a fat-tailed generalized $t$-distribution to one that gradually approaches the standard semicircle law for $H \gg H_c$. Our analytical results, based on scaling analysis and supported by extensive numerical simulations, provide clear predictions and uncover novel spectral regimes in random matrix theory. Our results connect techniques from statistical physics, percolation theory, and random matrix analysis, offering a new perspective on universality in correlated ensembles.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2604.22463"></a>
### [Quantum analog-encoding for correlated Gaussian vectors and their exponentiation with application to rough volatility](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.22463v1)

**Authors:** Tassa Thaksakronwong, Koichi Miyamoto

<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.22463_figures/2604.22463_fig5.jpg" alt="Figure 5">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 5.</b> Figure 5: Characteristics of standard fBM covariance matrix of increments</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.22463_figures/2604.22463_fig6.jpg" alt="Figure 6">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 6.</b> Figure 6: Characteristics of stationary fOU covariance matrix of increments</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.22463_figures/2604.22463_fig7.jpg" alt="Figure 7">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 7.</b> Figure 7: Comparison of overall cost required, when simulate using Σpv v.s. Σns</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Main problem.** Developing efficient quantum algorithms for the exact simulation and amplitude encoding of correlated Gaussian random vectors and their exponentiated forms, specifically for applications in rough volatility modeling.

**Main result.** The authors propose a quantum algorithmic framework that achieves sub-cubic complexity for preparing exponentiated Gaussian processes, providing a potential quantum advantage over classical Cholesky-based sampling methods.

**Method.** The approach utilizes block-encoding, Quantum Singular Value Transformation (QSVT), and Quantum Amplitude Estimation (QAE) to prepare and manipulate quantum states representing Gaussian paths.

**Summary.** This paper presents a new quantum algorithmic framework for the amplitude encoding of correlated Gaussian vectors and their exponentiated versions. By using techniques like block-encoding and QSVT, the authors demonstrate that quantum computers can potentially simulate complex Gaussian processes, such as fractional Brownian motion, with sub-cubic complexity. This provides a foundational primitive for quantum-enhanced financial modeling, specifically for rough volatility models like the rough Bergomi process. The work bridges quantum computing primitives with high-dimensional stochastic processes used in quantitative finance.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Model / system.** The mathematical models include multivariate Gaussian distributions, Riemann-Liouville fractional Brownian motion, and stationary fractional Ornstein-Uhlenbeck processes, applied to the rough Bergomi financial volatility model.

**Key observables.** Integrated variance, integrated volatility, and the norm of the prepared Gaussian vectors.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Hurst index (H), Frobenius norm of the covariance matrix, condition number (kappa), and maximal eigenvalue (lambda_max).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumes the availability of an efficient O(polylog N) quantum data loader for the covariance matrix and that eigenvalue/condition number estimates are available within bounded factors.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of Gaussian vector simulation, develops the block-encoding and state preparation algorithms, provides a resource and complexity analysis, applies the framework to rough volatility models (rBergomi), and concludes with mathematical proofs for the covariance structures in the appendix.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum computing may speed up numerical problems involving large matrices that are demanding for classical computers, and active research on this possibility is ongoing. In this work, we propose quantum algorithms for the exact simulation of a normalised correlated Gaussian random vector $|x\rangle=\vec{x}/\lVert\vec{x}\rVert$, $\vec{x}\sim\mathcal{N}(0,Σ)$, and its exponentiation $|e^{\vec{x}} \rangle= e^{\vec{x}}/\lVert e^{\vec{x}}\rVert$. When an $O(\mathrm{polylog} N)$-gate-depth quantum data loader for the covariance matrix $Σ\in\mathbb{R}^{N\times N}$ is available, preparing $|x\rangle$ and $|e^{\vec{x}}\rangle$ require $\widetilde{O}\left(\frac{\lVertΣ\rVert_F}{λ_{\max}}κ^{1.5}\right)$ and $\widetilde{O}\left(\lVert\vec{x}\rVert\frac{\lVertΣ\rVert_F}{λ_{\max}}κ^{1.5}\right)$ elementary gate depth respectively, where $\lVertΣ\rVert_F$, $λ_{\max}$, $κ$ denote the Frobenius norm, maximal eigenvalue, and condition number of $Σ$. Motivated by financial applications, we provide an end-to-end resource analysis when $\vec{x}$ represents a sample path of a Riemann-Liouville or standard fractional Brownian motion, or of a stationary fractional Ornstein-Uhlenbeck process. As a concrete example, we construct the quantum state encoding the rough Bergomi variance process and analyse the extraction of the integrated variance via quantum amplitude estimation. Under specific conditions, the dependence of $\lVertΣ\rVert_F/λ_{\max}$ and $κ$ on $N$ is small, and subcubic complexity in $N$ is achieved, indicating a quantum advantage over classical Cholesky-based sampling methods. To our knowledge, this constitutes the first quantum algorithmic framework for the amplitude encoding of exponentiated Gaussian processes, providing foundational primitives for quantum-enhanced financial modelling.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.22664"></a>
### [Quantum Circuit Partitioning For Effective Utilization of Quantum Resources](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.22664v1)

**Authors:** Connor Howe, Cristina Radian, Justin Woodring, Vardaan Sahgal, Brian J. McDermott  
**Type:** both · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.22664v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.22664_figures/2604.22664_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Fig. 1: Quantum circuit cutting workflow. A circuit is partitioned into smaller subcircuits at selected cut locations, executed independently on available quantum processing units (QPUs), and recombined via classical post-processing to reconstruct the original circuit output.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.22664_figures/2604.22664_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> Fig. 3: Family-level ∆MAE for fitv3 relative to the no-cut baseline across all three circuit families. Positive values indi- cate worse performance than direct execution, while negative values indicate improvement.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.22664_figures/2604.22664_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> Fig. 4: Difference in MAE between fitv3 and the no-cut baseline as a function of qubit count for each circuit family.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.22664_figures/2604.22664_fig4.jpg" alt="Figure 4">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> Fig. 5: Fraction of repeated runs for which fitv3 achieves lower MAE than the no-cut baseline as a function of qubit count for each circuit family.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Main problem.** The difficulty of executing large, high-performance quantum circuits on NISQ hardware due to high error rates and limited qubit counts, specifically identifying which circuit classes benefit most from circuit partitioning.

**Main result.** The custom 'fitv3' heuristic can reduce error by up to 55% and improve fidelity for GHZ circuits, though partitioning can degrade performance for brickwork circuits at larger scales.

**Method.** A comparison of three strategies: a no-cut baseline, Qiskit's automatic cut-finding, and a custom budget-aware heuristic (fitv3) using a DAG-to-weighted-graph transformation and hardware-aware qubit mapping.

**Summary.** This paper investigates quantum circuit partitioning (circuit cutting) as a way to run large circuits on small, noisy quantum hardware. The authors propose a new, budget-aware heuristic called 'fitv3' that optimizes the trade-off between error reduction and the exponential sampling overhead of reconstruction. They demonstrate that while partitioning is not a universal solution, it significantly improves fidelity for specific structured circuits like GHZ and QFT. The work provides a practical framework for utilizing distributed quantum computing resources more effectively.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Model / system.** Noisy simulation using Qiskit Aer, modeled after the IBM 'ibm_brisbane' superconducting hardware profile, testing circuit families including GHZ, QFT, brickwork, and random circuits.

**Key observables.** Mean Absolute Error (MAE) of expectation values and overall output fidelity, specifically for z_magnetization and GHZ stabilizers.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Circuit sizes ranging from 4 to 16 qubits, maximum subcircuit width of 4 qubits, and a maximum sampling overhead cap of 10^8.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The evaluation assumes a static snapshot of hardware calibration data and focuses on wire cuts rather than gate cuts.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the circuit cutting workflow; Figure 2 compares MAE across different strategies; Figure 5 shows the 'win-rate' of the custom method against the baseline as a function of qubit count.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of hardware constraints, reviews existing circuit cutting literature, presents the proposed fitv3 heuristic and integrated pipeline, details the simulation setup and circuit families, presents comparative results across different metrics, and concludes with limitations and future work.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Near-term hardware is constrained by high error rates, small qubit counts, and relatively low output fidelity, making the execution of large, high performance quantum circuits difficult. Circuit partitioning (or circuit cutting) has emerged as a promising approach to circumvent these limitations by decomposing circuits into smaller subcircuits at two-qubit interaction points. However, it remains unclear which classes of circuits benefit the most from partitioning and under what hardware conditions it is most effective. In this work, we evaluate the suitability of quantum circuits for partitioning from three perspectives: improving fidelity, enabling distributed execution, and scaling to larger circuit sizes. Specifically, we compare uncut circuit execution against two circuit partitioning approaches: Qiskit's automatic cut finding technique and a custom performance optimized circuit cutting method. We also measure these across GHZ, QFT, brickwork, and random quantum circuits ranging from 4 to 14 qubits, using mean absolute error of expectation values and overall output fidelity. Our results show that partitioning benefits larger, highly interconnected circuits, with our custom method reducing error by up to 55\% and improve fidelity for GHZ circuits, but degrading performance for brickwork circuits at larger scales.

</details>

**Main problem.** The study investigates whether an antiferromagnetic Chern insulator (AFCI) phase can emerge in the Kane-Mele-Hubbard model, addressing the controversy between previous mean-field and quantum Monte Carlo results.

**Main result.** The authors provide evidence for the existence of an AFCI phase (C=1) where Hubbard-induced antiferromagnetic perturbations spontaneously break time-reversal symmetry, and they propose a modified numerical scheme to accurately calculate the quantized Chern number.

**Method.** The study uses Exact Diagonalization (ED) on a 12A cluster, Twisted Boundary Conditions (TBC) to evaluate topological invariants, and Mean-Field theory to provide physical insight into the effective Dirac masses.

**Summary.** This paper investigates the emergence of an antiferromagnetic Chern insulator phase in the Kane-Mele-Hubbard model. By using exact diagonalization and a new computational scheme to handle level crossings, the authors demonstrate that Hubbard interactions can drive a transition that breaks time-reversal symmetry. The results resolve long-standing discrepancies in the literature regarding the stability of this phase. This work is significant for understanding how electron correlations can induce topological transitions in systems with intrinsic spin-orbit coupling.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Model / system.** The Kane-Mele-Hubbard (KMH) model on a honeycomb lattice at half-filling, including nearest-neighbor hopping, intrinsic spin-orbit coupling, a staggered sublattice potential, and onsite repulsive Hubbard interaction.

**Key observables.** Chern number (C), spin Chern number (Cs), excitation gap, fidelity susceptibility, and spin-density-wave (SDW) and charge-density-wave (CDW) structure factors.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Hubbard interaction strength (U), sublattice potential (Delta_AB), and intrinsic spin-orbit coupling strength (t2).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study is limited by finite-size effects inherent to Exact Diagonalization on small clusters.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the ED phase diagram; Figure 2 displays the excitation gap and topological transitions; Figure 7 shows the phase diagram with mass imbalance; Figure 10 and 11 present Mean-Field phase diagrams and spin-resolved effective potentials.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the scientific problem and model, presents the ED phase diagram and identification of the AFCI phase, details a modified numerical algorithm for Chern number calculation, explores the stability of the phase under mass imbalance, and provides Mean-Field analysis for physical interpretation.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The emergence of the antiferromagnetic (AFM) Chern insulator (AFCI) phase in the Kane-Mele-Hubbard (KMH) model with a finite sublattice potential is investigated. The AFCI, characterized by AFM correlations coexisting with quantized Hall conductance, has long raised the question of whether it can exist in the KMH model that respects time-reversal symmetry (TRS). Using exact diagonalization, we analyze the excitation gap, anisotropic AFM correlations along the $z$ axis and in the $xy$ plane, and the fidelity susceptibility under twisted boundary conditions, all of which provide consistent evidence for the AFCI phase. In particular, our numerical evaluation on the (spin) Chern number reveals a breakdown of adiabatic continuity in the twist-angle space, indicating an instability toward TRS breaking driven by Hubbard-induced AFM perturbations. A modified computational scheme is further proposed, which yields a robust quantized Chern number $C=1$ within this phase.

</details>

### [Valley enhanced Rabi frequency in n-type planar Silicon-MOS quantum dot](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.22547v1)

**Authors:** Xunyao Luo, Xander Peetroons, Tsung-Yeh Yang, Ruben M. Otxoa, Normann Mertig, Sofie Beyne, Julien Jussot, Yosuke Shimura, Clement Godfrin, Bart Raes, Roy Li, Roger Loo, Sylvain Baudot, Stefan Kubicek, Shuchi Kaushik, Danny Wan, Kristiaan De Greve, Takuma Kuno, Takeru Utsugi, Noriyuki Lee, Itaru Yanagi, Toshiyuki Mine, Satoshi Muraoka, Hideo Arimoto, Shinichi Saito, Digh Hisamoto, Ryuta Tsuchiya, Hiroyuki Mizuno, Charles Smith, Andrew Ramsay  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.22547v1>

The damping time suggests an optimum value, a trade-off between greater dynamical decoupling from noise in the qubit frequency at high Rabi frequencies, and greater susceptibility of the Rabi frequency to charge noise induced fluctuations in the valley-splitting.</figcaption>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates how to achieve fast, all-electrical control of electron spins in silicon quantum dots without the need for micromagnets, by leveraging spin-valley hybridization.

**Main result.** The researchers observed a >20-fold enhancement of the Rabi frequency near a valley level anti-crossing, attributed to an electric-dipole transition activated by inter-valley spin coupling.

**Method.** Electron Spin Resonance (ESR) spectroscopy was performed on a single-electron spin in a planar Si-MOS quantum dot using microwave pulses from an aluminum antenna and RF-SET charge sensing for readout.

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that spin-valley coupling in planar silicon quantum dots can be used to significantly enhance the Rabi frequency for spin manipulation. By operating near a valley level anti-crossing, the researchers achieved an electric-dipole-driven transition that is much stronger than traditional magnetic-dipole ESR. They also characterized the anisotropy of the g-factor and the inter-valley spin coupling. This provides a promising pathway for fast, all-electrical spin control in silicon-based quantum computing without requiring integrated micromagnets.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Model / system.** A single-electron spin in a planar n-type Si-MOS double quantum dot on an SOI wafer, modeled as a four-state system comprising two spin and two valley states.

**Key observables.** Rabi frequency enhancement, valley splitting, g-factor anisotropy (difference and mean), and spin-relaxation rate (hot-spot).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Valley splitting of ~66 ueV, electron temperature of ~120 mK, and an electric-dipole drive strength approximately 200 times stronger than the magnetic-dipole drive.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The model assumes the primary g-factor tensor difference lies in off-diagonal terms and that the inter-valley spin-orbit field is in-plane.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows the device schematic and ESR spectra; Fig 5 illustrates spin relaxation visibility as a function of magnetic field.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of spin control, describes the Si-MOS device architecture, presents ESR spectroscopy results near the anti-crossing, analyzes the anisotropy of the g-factor and spin-valley coupling, and concludes with implications for all-electrical spin control.

**Why it may be interesting.** The discovery of a mechanism for massive enhancement of Rabi frequency via electric-dipole transitions in a multi-level system is highly relevant to the development of fast, high-fidelity control protocols in multi-level quantum systems.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Electron spin resonance spectroscopy (ESR) of a single electron in planar Si-MOS quantum dot is reported in the vicinity of a valley level anti-crossing. A number of one and two-photon resonances are observed due to mixing of magnetic spin-flip and electric valley-flip transitions. This allows the reconstruction of the energy-level diagram of a four state system with two valley and two spin states. Near the anti-crossing, an enhancement of the Rabi frequency is observed. This is attributed to an electric-dipole transition activated by admixing of the upper energy level due to inter-valley spin coupling. The electric-dipole transition may be driven via capacitive coupling between the ESR antenna, and the confinement gate. To characterize spin-valley coupling responsible for the enhancement, we measure the anisotropy of the g-factor difference between the two valley states, the mean g-factor and the inter-valley spin coupling for both in and out-of-plane magnetic fields. The inter-valley spin coupling is strongly modulated by the direction of the B-field, and is strongest for out-of-plane B-field, consistent with an in-plane spin-valley field. In principle, this strong Electric dipole spin resonance (EDSR) effect could be utilized for fast all-electrical spin control in small-scale devices.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.22408"></a>
### [Odd pathways speed up self-assembly](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.22408v1)

**Authors:** Dawid Dopierała, Luca Cocconi, Robert L. Jack, Anton Souslov  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.22408v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `dissipative systems` **3/5** · 🔥 `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **2/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **2/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.22408_figures/2604.22408_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. Transverse forces as effective temperature. How do transverse forces affect self-assembly? a, Abstraction of transverse forces: pairs of metallodielectric (Janus) colloids driven by a uniform AC electric field [18], Kilobots [19], and ball-and- spring model. Battery represents energy injection, which results in transverse forces (red arrows) proportional to ka(r −r0). b, Abstraction of self-assembly: thermal noise leads to the assembly of target structures, for example in colloidomers [20]. Resulting phenomenology: c, Steady-state probability distribution for two-particle system (top, inset) remains the same with and without transverse forces (top). However, transverse forces...</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.22408_figures/2604.22408_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> FIG. 2. Activity-enhanced folding. a, Steady-state probability distribution of the angles β and γ for a rhombic configuration of four springs. Horizontal and vertical lines indicate transitions via wedge states, while diagonals correspond to rhombus configurations. Zoom of the first quadrant (right) shows that, starting from the square configuration (centre of the diagonal), the system first assembles into a line with the length of two springs. In the steady state, the system is biased towards near-zero β configurations (left). (T = 0.001, ka = 0) b, Sample trajectories of the system signed area A and histograms of folding times over many runs (shaded). Transverse forces reduce the folding...</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.22408_figures/2604.22408_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> FIG. 3. Acceleration of transitions between metastable states. a, Ladder (top) and chevron (bottom) are metastable configurations of the double-square system enforced by the Morse interaction (halos indicate interaction range; faded halos denote effectively non-interacting particles). A sample trajectory (b, ka = 2, T = 0.005) shows repeated transitions between ladder (above the blue dotted line) and chevron (between the red dotted lines). c, Transverse forces increase the transition rates R for ladder→chevron (top) and chevron→ladder (middle), while their ratio remains constant (bottom), indicating probability invariance. d, Accelerated stabilization in a system of non-interacting...</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Main problem.** How to accelerate the kinetics of self-assembly and transition between metastable states without altering the equilibrium Boltzmann distribution or distorting the final target structures.

**Main result.** Non-reciprocal (odd) interactions induce probability currents that accelerate assembly via mode coupling and increased effective mobility, though this comes at the cost of increased power dissipation.

**Method.** Analytical derivation using Langevin and Fokker-Planck equations, adiabatic elimination of fast modes, and numerical simulations of stochastic dynamics.

**Summary.** The paper investigates how 'odd' or non-reciprocal forces can be used to speed up the self-assembly of mechanical structures. It demonstrates that these forces can drive particles across energy barriers to accelerate transitions without changing the final equilibrium state. The authors identify a fundamental trade-off between the speed of assembly and the energy dissipated by the active forces. This suggests a 'catalytic' strategy where active forces are used transiently to drive assembly and then deactivated to maintain high-fidelity structures.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Model / system.** Mechanical networks of overdamped thermal colloids subject to non-conservative transverse forces, including specific models like two-particle odd springs, odd triangles, and four/six-particle square networks.

**Key observables.** Probability currents, transition rates (flip rates), assembly time, power dissipation, and steady-state probability distributions.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Non-reciprocal coupling strength (ka), spring constant (k), temperature (T), and angular potential strength (epsilon).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Low-temperature regime, separation of time scales between fast and slow modes, and small deformation approximations for certain analytical derivations.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows steady-state distributions, transition rates, and assembly trajectories; Figure 2 illustrates the path from square to line; Figure A1 displays elastic eigenmodes of the triangle.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of kinetic bottlenecks, presents a framework for non-reciprocal forces, analyzes specific small-scale models (2, 3, and 4-particle systems), explores complex assembly (double-square), and concludes with a discussion on the energetic trade-offs and catalytic applications.

**Why it may be interesting.** The study of non-reciprocal dynamics and the decoupling of kinetics from thermodynamics is highly relevant to open quantum systems and the control of non-equilibrium steady states.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Active self-assembly can bypass equilibrium bottlenecks through external energy injection. However, generic driving typically distorts target structures and requires sustained energy input even after assembly is complete. Here, we investigate a class of non-reciprocal interactions that accelerates assembly while preserving the equilibrium Boltzmann distribution. The probability currents induced by these odd interactions reshape fundamental processes, including activated barrier crossing, soft-mode relaxation, and transitions between metastable states. In particular, these currents enhance Arrhenius rates by driving particles across otherwise inaccessible free-energy barriers. We show that this acceleration arises from an effective increase in the mobility of the reaction coordinate, mediated by non-reciprocal coupling between mechanical modes. In turn, we discover a trade-off between kinetic acceleration and power dissipation when active forces are engaged. Our results suggest a route to energy-efficient, high-fidelity self-assembly via active catalysts that transiently accelerate relaxation toward equilibrium targets and deactivate upon reaching the desired state.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.22371"></a>
### [Covariant Onsager and Onsager-Machlup principles for active and inertial dynamics](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.22371v1)

**Authors:** Kento Yasuda, Bin Zheng, Zhongqiang Xiong, Zhanglin Hou, Kenta Ishimoto, Xinpeng Xu, David Andelman, Shigeyuki Komura  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.22371v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5** · `dissipative systems` **2/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2604.22371_figures/2604.22371_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. Entropy balance for a system coupled to a heat bath at temperature T. The meso-state of the system is described by q, while the microscopic configuration is represented by x with Hamiltonian H(x). The system entropy is decomposed as Ssys = Smic + Smes. If the system exchanges heat Q with the bath and work W with the surroundings, the bath entropy change is ∆Sbath = Q/T, and the total entropy production is given by σ = ∆Ssys + ∆Sbath.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Main problem.** Existing formulations of the Onsager and Onsager-Machlup principles lack covariance, making them inconsistent under coordinate transformations and unable to account for inertial effects in active systems.

**Main result.** The authors develop a covariant framework for the Onsager and Onsager-Machlup principles that ensures geometric consistency under coordinate transformations and successfully incorporates both active forces and inertial dynamics.

**Method.** The study uses a variational approach involving the minimization of the Rayleighian and the Onsager-Machlup functional, utilizing a metric tensor and covariant acceleration to maintain invariance.

**Summary.** This paper addresses the mathematical inconsistency of existing variational principles for dissipative dynamics under coordinate transformations. The authors introduce a covariant formulation of the Onsager and Onsager-Machlup principles that remains invariant under point transformations. This new framework successfully integrates active (non-equilibrium) forces and inertial effects into a unified geometric structure. The resulting theory is shown to be consistent with the principles of stochastic thermodynamics and the detailed fluctuation theorem.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Model / system.** The framework applies to mesoscopic systems in contact with a heat bath, specifically addressing active systems (driven out of equilibrium) and inertial systems (including kinetic energy and mass). Examples include 3D active Brownian particles and 2D particles in polar coordinates.

**Key observables.** Non-equilibrium free energy, entropy production, path probability density, internal energy, and work/heat exchange.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Temperature (T), friction/resistance tensor (zeta), inertia tensor (mu), and active power/forces (X).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The system is in a non-equilibrium state; meso-state variables are uniquely determined by micro-states; the heat bath is at a constant temperature; and transformations are smooth, one-to-one point transformations.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows an entropy balance diagram illustrating the decomposition of system entropy into micro and meso parts, and the relationship between heat, work, and bath entropy change.

**Paper structure.** The paper first identifies the lack of covariance in existing principles, then develops a covariant formulation for active systems, extends this to include thermal fluctuations via the Onsager-Machlup principle, incorporates inertia using covariant acceleration, and validates the theory with specific models (active Brownian particles and polar coordinates) and thermodynamic consistency proofs.

**Why it may be interesting.** This is highly relevant for researchers in open quantum systems and many-body dynamics as it provides a mathematically rigorous, coordinate-invariant way to describe the stochastic trajectories and thermodynamics of driven, dissipative, and inertial systems.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The Onsager principle provides a variational route to the phenomenological equations of dissipative dynamics through the minimization of the Rayleighian. We develop a covariant formulation of the Onsager principle for active systems, ensuring geometric consistency under coordinate transformations. To further incorporate thermal fluctuations, we formulate the Onsager-Machlup principle for active systems by considering the Onsager-Machlup functional and the corresponding path probability for stochastic trajectories. Requiring that the path probability obeys the detailed fluctuation theorem, we show that the extended Onsager-Machlup theory is consistent with stochastic thermodynamics. Moreover, we incorporate inertia into the variational framework and show that the proper covariant equations follow when the covariant acceleration is held fixed during the variation.

</details>

### [Extended Haldane Model in The Dice Lattice: Multiple Flat-Band-Induced topological Transitions Revealed](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.22131v1)

The other parameters are set to 𝜙𝑎= 𝜋/2, 𝛼= 1, and 𝑡𝑐= 𝑡𝑎= 0.1𝑡.</figcaption>
</figure>

**Main problem.** The study investigates how breaking inversion and time-reversal symmetries in an extended Haldane model on a dice lattice induces multiple topological phase transitions.

**Main result.** The authors identify critical flux values at phi_c = pi/6 and 5pi/6 where gap closures occur, leading to changes in Chern numbers across all bands and the emergence of unquantized Hall conductivity plateaus.

**Method.** The researchers use a tight-binding Hamiltonian approach, deriving analytical expressions for quasi-energies and calculating Berry curvature, orbital magnetic moment, and Chern numbers via the TKNN index.

**Summary.** This paper explores a modified Haldane model on a dice lattice where independent control of magnetic fluxes breaks inversion symmetry. The study reveals that at specific critical flux values, the energy gaps close, triggering topological transitions that affect the valence, flat, and conduction bands. These transitions are characterized by changes in Chern numbers and the appearance of both quantized and unquantized Hall conductivity plateaus. The findings suggest a pathway for designing tunable, low-dissipation topological quantum devices.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Model / system.** An extended Haldane model on a dice lattice (a specific case of the alpha-T3 lattice where alpha=1), featuring modified next-nearest-neighbor hopping phases (phi_a and phi_c) that break inversion symmetry.

**Key observables.** Chern numbers (C0, C1, C2), Berry curvature, orbital magnetic moment, and anomalous Hall conductivity.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Fluxes phi_a and phi_c, lattice parameter alpha, hopping amplitudes t_a and t_c, and critical points at phi_c = pi/6 and 5pi/6.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The model assumes the NNN hops on sublattice B can be neglected due to high energy barriers; the analysis focuses on the low-energy regime.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows the lattice geometry, band structures, and high-symmetry paths; Fig 2 displays Berry curvature density plots; Fig 6 shows Chern number variations and Hall conductivity plateaus.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the modified lattice model and symmetry breaking, derives analytical quasi-energies, performs topological analysis via Berry curvature and Chern numbers, discusses transport properties like Hall conductivity, and proposes experimental implementations in cold atoms and van der Waals heterostructures.

**Why it may be interesting.** The paper explores the engineering of topological phases and transport properties through flux control, which is highly relevant to the development of reconfigurable topological devices and Floquet engineering in AMO systems.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

In this study, we examine the introduction of the Haldane model into the dice lattice by altering the flow between the next-nearest-neighbour sites. This breaks the lattice's inversion and time-reversal symmetries. We demonstrate the presence of point-charge particle symmetries at $φ^c=π/6$ and $5π/6$ and derive the analytical expression for quasi-energies. We demonstrate that a gap closure occurs at these critical points, inducing a topological transition. This is confirmed by calculating the Berry curvature and orbital magnetic moment. A topological analysis shows that the Chern numbers of the valence band $(ν=0)$, the flat band $(ν=1)$ and the conduction band $(ν=2)$ depend strongly on the relationship between the fluxes $φ^a $ and $φ^c$. When $φ^c = φ^a$, the Chern numbers are $(C_0, C_1, C_2) = (2, -2, 0)$ in the region $φ^c \in [0, π/6[$, and (0, 2, -2) in the region $φ^c\in ]5π/6, π]$. Conversely, when $φ^c \neq φ^a$, the topological invariants become $ (C_1, C_2) = (-1, -1)$ for $φ^c \in [0, π/6[$, and $(C_0, C_1, )= (1, 1)$ for $φ^c\in ]5π/6, π]$. These variations reflect topological phase transitions at the critical points $φ^c=π/6$ and $5π/6$, affecting all of the system's bands. Furthermore, the anomalous Hall conductivity exhibits a quantized plateau of 2$σ_{0}$, as well as an unquantized tilted plateau evolving from 1.50$σ_{0}$ to 1.25$σ_{0}$ at the same transition points. Controlling the flux allows topological transitions to be engineered and quantum transport in the dice lattice to be optimised, offering promising prospects for reconfigurable topological devices with low dissipation and robust quantum transport.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.22484"></a>

<img src="2604.22484_figures/2604.22484_fig10.jpg" alt="Figure 10">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 10.</b> FIG. 10. Magnon mode lineshape a+− 0 (q, ω) in hcp-Co at the degenerate hexagonal face of the BZ boundary (colored contour) overlaid by the corresponding Kohn-Sham lineshape a+− KS,0(q, ω) (greyscale line contour) and extracted coherent magnon peak frequencies (blue crosses). For additional de- tails, please refer to Fig. 9.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Main problem.** The difficulty in interpreting complex magnetic excitation spectra in itinerant ferromagnets, specifically distinguishing between coherent magnons and incoherent Stoner excitations, and resolving numerical 'Goldstone gap' errors in TDDFT.

**Main result.** The authors developed a novel LR-TDDFT implementation that eliminates the Goldstone gap error and provides a method to classify magnons as coherent or incoherent using the self-enhancement function, revealing features like branch coexistence in Fe and valley magnons in Ni.

**Method.** A novel implementation of linear response time-dependent density functional theory (LR-TDDFT) within the PAW method, utilizing eigendecomposition of the spectral function and the self-enhancement function.

**Summary.** This paper presents a new way to accurately calculate and classify magnetic excitations (magnons) in metals like iron and nickel. By improving the numerical implementation of TDDFT, the authors successfully eliminate non-physical energy gaps and can distinguish between stable, coherent magnons and decaying, incoherent excitations. The work reveals complex phenomena such as the coexistence of multiple magnon branches and the existence of 'valley magnons' in nickel, providing a more precise theoretical benchmark for neutron scattering experiments.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Model / system.** Elemental itinerant ferromagnets including bcc-Fe, fcc-Ni, fcc-Co, and hcp-Co, modeled using the Adiabatic Local Density Approximation (ALDA) within a TDDFT framework.

**Key observables.** Transverse magnetic susceptibility, scattering function, self-enhancement function, magnon dispersion, and Stoner continuum/gap.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Wave vectors (q), frequency (omega), exchange splitting, and plane-wave energy cutoffs.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The use of the Adiabatic Local Density Approximation (ALDA) for the exchange-correlation kernel and the focus on collinear magnetic systems without spin-orbit coupling.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the physics of the self-enhancement function; Figure 2 shows the convergence of the Goldstone gap error; Figure 3 compares many-body vs. Kohn-Sham Stoner spectra; Figure 4 shows eigenvalues and magnon lineshapes for Co; Figure 14-15 show magnon enhancement and branch coexistence in Fe; Figure 16 shows the Ni spectrum; Figure 18-19 show convergence of peak frequencies.

**Paper structure.** Introduction to the problem of itinerant magnetism; description of the new LR-TDDFT implementation and error elimination strategy; application to Fe, Ni, and Co; classification of excitations via the self-enhancement function; discussion of convergence and basis set requirements; conclusion on implications for magnetic properties.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The magnetic excitation spectrum of itinerant magnets exhibits rich and complex spectral features that often complicate interpretation of the underlying physics. For perturbations in the long wavelength limit, one obtains a well defined pole at zero frequency in the spectral function, the Goldstone magnon. However, for optical modes and finite wavevectors, the magnon spectrum may become damped, exhibit branching, or be completely washed out. In the present work, we show how the physical mechanism of all such features can be understood from careful analysis of the eigenmodes of the many-body spectral function. We perform first principles computations of elemental itinerant ferromagnets using a novel implementation of the linear response time-dependent density functional theory (LR-TDDFT) framework and classify the collective nature of individual spectral features based on the self-enhancement function, the product of the noninteracting Kohn-Sham susceptibility and the exchange-correlation kernel. In particular, we distinguish between coherent and incoherent collective excitations, depending on whether the real part of the self-enhancement function crosses unity at the spectral peak of the magnon, which may or may not be subject to Landau damping as quantified by the imaginary part. Classifying the computed magnon spectra accordingly, we observe coexistence of coherent magnon branches in bcc-Fe, as well as decoherence of the primary magnon branch in fcc-Ni for wave vectors near the BZ boundary where incoherent valley magnons instead carry substantial spectral weight. The analysis also naturally leads to a definition of the many-body Stoner spectrum and allows us to quantify the binding energy of the Stoner pair excitations.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.22148"></a>
### [Harnessing Plasmonic Heating For Switching In Antiferromagnets](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.22148v1)

**Main problem.** The challenge of high energy consumption and heat waste in traditional spintronic devices, specifically the difficulty of efficiently and reversibly switching antiferromagnetic (AFM) domains.

**Main result.** Demonstrated that controlled nanoscale plasmonic heating can reversibly switch AFM domains with energy consumption 3 to 6 orders of magnitude lower than current-driven methods by using polarization-dependent thermal strain.

**Method.** Multi-physics numerical simulations using COMSOL Multiphysics (Maxwell's equations, heat diffusion, and thermal-elastic equations) combined with custom micromagnetic simulations.

**Summary.** This paper proposes a paradigm shift in spintronics by using heat as a functional resource rather than a waste product. By using plasmonic heating in a gold nanostructure, the researchers show that light polarization can control thermal strain to switch antiferromagnetic domains. This method achieves switching energies significantly lower than traditional electrical methods. The approach offers a highly efficient, scalable path for all-optical magnetic information processing.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Model / system.** A hybrid nanostructure consisting of a metallic (gold) square frame/nanowire integrated with an antiferromagnetic (NiO) thin film.

**Key observables.** Temperature distribution, strain field components (u_xx, u_yy), absorption cross-section, and the orientation of the Néel vector.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Switching energy ~1 nJ; NiO Néel temperature ~525 K; 1 ns laser pulse duration; 1.2 um wavelength; thermal diffusion length ~100 nm.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The mechanism is robust at operating temperatures where temperature-dependent changes in NiO's thermal expansion and density are minimal (~1%).

**Figures summary.** Fig 1: Schematic of the hybrid structure and switching concept. Fig 2: Energy landscape and lattice deformation illustration. Fig 3: Wavelength dependence of temperature/absorption and strain anisotropy. Fig 4: Temporal evolution of strain and temperature. Fig 5: Radial dependence of temperature, displacement, and strain.

**Paper structure.** Introduction of the heat-as-a-resource paradigm, description of the hybrid Au/NiO system, detailed multi-physics simulation workflow, analysis of polarization-dependent strain generation, demonstration of reversible switching, and discussion of scalability and robustness.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Heat waste is a bottleneck in the development of green information technologies and much effort has been devoted to suppress the heating effect in both electronic and spintronic devices. Here we take an alternative approach and show that controllable heating at the nanoscale can actually benefit information processing. In particular, we study a hybrid nanostructure consisting of a metallic square frame and an antiferromagnetic (AFM) thin film and show that the plasmonic heating can reversibly switch two perpendicularly-oriented AFM domains without the assistance of magnetic fields and electric currents. The required switching energy is at the order 1 nJ, three to six orders of magnitude lower than the current-driven AFM switching. The physical mechanism arises from the thermal-induced strain fields inside the frame, which couple to and manipulate the magnetic orientation via magnetoelastic effect. The strain field direction can be well controlled by selectively exciting the longitudinal and transverse plasmon modes by varying the polarization of the waves, which readily allows for a reversible switching of the AFM vector. Our findings provide tremendous opportunities for optically manipulating the magnetism with ultralow energy consumption and may further promote the interdisciplinary study of photonics, acoustics and spintronics.

</details>

### [Microscopic Modeling of Surface Roughness Scattering in Inversion Layers of MOSFETs Based on Ando's Linear Model](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.22155v1)

**Authors:** Nobuyuki Sano  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.22155v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5**

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the discrepancy between conventional Ando-style linear models for surface roughness (SR) scattering and experimental measurements, specifically the mismatch in roughness parameters and the inadequacy of Fermi's golden rule in high-field regimes.

**Main result.** The proposed microscopic model reconciles theoretical and experimental roughness parameters and demonstrates that a self-consistent Green's function approach predicts higher electron mobility than the conventional Fermi's golden rule, especially at low energies and high effective fields.

**Method.** The authors use a Green's function scheme and the Born approximation to derive a self-consistent scattering rate, treating the interface roughness position as a stochastic variable with a 2D Gaussian probability density.

**Summary.** This paper proposes a new microscopic model for surface roughness scattering in MOSFET inversion layers to resolve long-standing discrepancies between theory and experiment. By treating the interface roughness position as a stochastic variable, the authors show that the scattering rate is intrinsically nonlocal. They demonstrate that using a self-consistent Green's function approach, rather than the standard Fermi's golden rule, leads to more accurate predictions of electron mobility in high-field, low-energy regimes. This approach successfully aligns theoretical roughness parameters with those measured via TEM.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Model / system.** The system is the inversion layer of bulk Si-MOSFETs (specifically Si (100)) under strong inversion, modeled using a quantum-mechanical framework that accounts for the discontinuity of electrostatic potential and wave-function derivatives at the semiconductor/dielectric interface.

**Key observables.** Retarded self-energy, SR scattering rate, and SR-limited electron mobility.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Effective electric field (F_eff), electron energy (E), sheet density (N_s), roughness height (Delta_A), and correlation length (Lambda_A).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The model assumes a 2D Gaussian probability density for roughness position, neglects correlations between roughness at different atomic sites, and uses Ando's asymptotic expression for subband wave-functions.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows the broadening/narrowing of the Green's function integrand with field and energy; Fig 2 compares scattering rates; Fig 3 compares mobility predictions (self-consistent vs. FGR) and shows the contribution of different scattering mechanisms (SR vs. phonon).

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the discrepancy in existing models, proposes a microscopic stochastic model for interface roughness, derives the self-consistent scattering rate using Green's functions, compares the results to the conventional Fermi's golden rule, and validates the model against experimental mobility data.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

A microscopic model of surface roughness (SR) scattering in inversion layers of bulk-MOSFETs based on Ando's linear model is proposed. Taking into account the stochastic nature of roughness position induced by discontinuity of the spatial derivatives of electrostatic potential and wave-function at the semiconductor/dielectric interface, a probability density of roughness position is introduced at each atomic site. The roughness parameters in the proposed model are consistent with those from experiments, and thus, there is no discrepancy between theory and experiment. The SR scattering rate is then derived by using the Green's function scheme, and we find that the scattering rate is intrinsically nonlocal (nondiagonal) with respect to subband indices and position. In addition, the self-consistent scattering rate greatly deviates from those obtained by Fermi's golden rule in the regimes of strong effective fields and low electron energies. As a result, the conventional model tends to predict smaller SR-limited mobility.

</details>

<a id="paper-2604.22393"></a>
### [Nature of point defects in bulk hexagonal diamond](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.22393v1)

**Authors:** Ling Zhu, Xuanxuan Zhang, Guliqinayi Alimu, Chen-Min Dai, Chunlan Ma, Zenghua Cai

**Main problem.** The study aims to systematically investigate the nature, stability, and electronic properties of intrinsic and extrinsic point defects and defect complexes in bulk hexagonal diamond (HD) for applications in conductivity engineering and quantum technologies.

**Main result.** The researchers identified Boron as an effective p-type acceptor and Nitrogen/Phosphorus as effective n-type donors, while also discovering that certain complexes like VC, MgC, and XV exhibit multiple spin and charge states suitable for use as color centers/qubits.

**Method.** First-principles calculations using Density Functional Theory (DFT) with the VASP code, employing the HSE06 hybrid functional and the FNV scheme for charge corrections.

**Summary.** This paper provides a comprehensive theoretical study of point defects in hexagonal diamond. It identifies key dopants for controlling electrical conductivity, specifically Boron for p-type and Nitrogen/Phosphorus for n-type. Crucially, it highlights several defect types that could serve as stable color centers for quantum computing. The work establishes a roadmap for engineering the electronic and quantum properties of this carbon allotrope.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Model / system.** The physical system is bulk hexagonal diamond (HD), an exotic carbon allotrope (lonsdaleite) with an ABAB stacking sequence, featuring both intrinsic (vacancies, interstitials) and extrinsic (Group II, III, IV, and V) defects.

**Key observables.** Defect formation energies, transition energy levels (TELs), charge states, spin polarization, and binding energies of defect complexes.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Chemical potentials under C-rich conditions, Fermi level position, and band gap width (calculated at 4-4.62 eV).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Calculations were performed under C-rich conditions and assumed the stability of the HD phase without secondary phase formation.

**Figures summary.** Figure 2 shows formation energies of various XV complexes; Figure 5 illustrates structural distortions in P-related complexes; Figure 6 displays calculated transition energy levels within the HD band gap.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the properties of HD, details the DFT computational methodology, presents a systematic investigation of intrinsic defects, moves to extrinsic dopants by group, analyzes defect complexes, and concludes with implications for quantum and electronic applications.

**Why it may be interesting.** The identification of specific defect complexes (VC, MgC, XV) that host multiple spin and charge states within the band gap is highly relevant for the development of solid-state color centers for quantum information processing.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Hexagonal diamond (HD), an exotic carbon allotrope recently synthesized in bulk form, exhibits superior mechanical properties compared to cubic diamond (CD) and holds promise for advanced industrial and quantum applications. Using first-principles calcu-lations, we systematically investigate intrinsic defects, extrinsic dopants, and defect complexes in HD. Our study shows that VC dominates intrinsic conductivity, while Ci is unstable. Among extrinsic dopants, boron acts as a benign acceptor enhancing p-type conductivity, whereas nitrogen and phosphorus serve as effective donors for n-type conductivity. Group II and Group IV dopants, however, introduce high formation energies or neutral charge states with limited impact. Furthermore, VC, MgC and XV defect com-plexes display multiple spin and charge states within the HD band gap, highlighting their potential as color centers for hosting qubits. These results not only clarify the defect physics of HD but also demonstrate its broader implications for conductivity engineering and quantum technologies.

</details>

### [Non-volatile superconducting tunnelling magnetoresistance memory enabled by exchange-field gap engineering](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.22214v1)

**Authors:** Sonam Bhakat, Pushpak Banerjee, Ahmedullah Aziz, Jackson Miller, Avradeep Pal

**Main problem.** The lack of scalable, energy-efficient, and non-volatile memory technologies compatible with superconducting and quantum computing systems operating below 4K.

**Main result.** Demonstration of a superconducting tunneling magnetoresistance device that achieves non-volatile, temperature-independent switching of the superconducting energy gap via exchange-field engineering.

**Method.** The researchers used ultra-high vacuum sputtering to grow a multi-layered thin film stack and employed multi-step lithography to create mesa-type junctions, followed by tunneling spectroscopy and magneto-transport measurements.

**Summary.** This paper presents a new type of superconducting memory element that uses magnetic fields to tune the superconducting energy gap. Unlike previous technologies that only work in a narrow temperature range, this device remains functional and non-volatile across the entire superconducting temperature range. It operates with extremely low power consumption (nanowatts) and is compatible with existing niobium-based superconducting logic. This makes it a promising candidate for building scalable, energy-efficient hardware for quantum and neuromorphic computing.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Model / system.** A vertical current-perpendicular-to-plane (CPP) device architecture consisting of a de Gennes spin valve integrated with a superconducting tunnel junction, specifically a GdN/Nb/GdN/Al-AlOx/Nb multilayer stack.

**Key observables.** Superconducting energy gap (delta), Quasiparticle Tunneling Magnetoresistance (QTMR), differential conductance (dI/dV), and transition temperature (Tc).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Operating temperatures down to 0.25K, nanowatt-level read power, zero standby dissipation, and 95% spin polarization.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The device is modeled as two parallel segments where the S1 electrode is subject to Zeeman splitting and spin-orbit scattering, and the S2 electrode acts as a stable, magnetically decoupled reference.

**Figures summary.** Figure 2 shows CIP resistance vs. magnetic field and temperature; Figure 4c shows bias-dependent QTMR.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the bottleneck in cryogenic hardware, describes the device architecture and fabrication, presents magneto-transport and tunneling spectroscopy results, validates the mechanism through numerical modeling of the density of states, and discusses applications in neuromorphic and cryogenic computing.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Scalable, low-dissipation memory operating below 4 K is a critical requirement for superconducting and quantum computing systems. Existing cryogenic memory technologies rely on CMOS derivatives or hybrid architectures that incur leakage, refresh overhead or limited compatibility with superconducting logic. Here we demonstrate a superconducting tunnelling magnetoresistance device that functions as a non-volatile cryogenic memory element across the full superconducting temperature range. By integrating a de Gennes spin valve with a superconducting tunnel junction in a current perpendicular-to-plane geometry, we realise exchange-field control of the superconducting energy gap. This produces two magnetically switchable gap voltages and robust quasiparticle tunnelling magnetoresistance down to 0.25 K.The device operates at millivolt bias with nanowatt-level read power and zero standby dissipation. Its vertical junction architecture and Nb-based materials platform enable compatibility with superconducting logic and scalable cryogenic memory arrays.

</details>

### [Accurate calculation of Wannier centers, position matrix, and composite operators using translationally equivariant and higher-order finite differences](http://arxiv.org/abs/2604.22614v1)

**Authors:** Jae-Mo Lihm, Minsu Ghim, Seung-Ju Hong, Cheol-Hwan Park

**Main problem.** Standard finite-difference approximations for position and composite operators in Wannier interpolation suffer from slow convergence, broken translational equivariance, and violations of crystal symmetries.

**Main result.** The authors developed a translationally equivariant finite-difference (TEFD) scheme and a higher-order (HOFD) approach that restore system symmetries, ensure size-consistency, and significantly accelerate convergence of $k$-space derivatives.

**Method.** The work introduces a midpoint-based TEFD scheme to ensure equivariance and a higher-order HOFD method using weighted $k$-point grids to improve error scaling from $O(b^{-2})$ to $O(b^{-2N})$.

**Summary.** This paper presents improved numerical algorithms for Wannier interpolation used in first-principles calculations. By introducing translationally equivariant and higher-order finite-difference schemes, the authors solve long-standing issues regarding symmetry-breaking errors and slow convergence in calculating quantum geometric properties. The new methods are implemented in widely used open-source packages like Wannier90 and WannierBerri, making them immediately accessible for more accurate electronic structure simulations.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Model / system.** The methods are applied to periodic electronic systems (solids) described by Bloch wavefunctions and Wannier functions, with specific test cases including graphene, GeS, bcc Fe, CrO2, and GaAs.

**Key observables.** Wannier centers and spreads, electric polarization, orbital magnetization, spin Hall conductivity, and interband velocity matrix elements.

**Important parameters / regimes.** k-point grid density, finite-difference order, and translation shift parameters.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The derivation assumes the localization of Wannier functions and, for certain symmetry proofs, assumes a simplified case where one Wannier function transforms into exactly one other.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 compares S-FD vs. TEFD and HOFD schemes; Figure 2 shows Wannier center stability under shifts in GeS; Figure 4-6 demonstrate orbital magnetization and spin Hall conductivity convergence in Fe, CrO2, and GaAs; Figure 12-13 compare spin-velocity interpolation methods and convergence in GaAs.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by identifying errors in standard finite-difference methods, then introduces the TEFD and HOFD schemes, provides mathematical proofs for symmetry and size-consistency, demonstrates application to composite operators, and concludes with numerical validation on various materials and implementation details in open-source codes.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The momentum-space derivatives of Bloch wavefunctions are essential for studying quantum geometry and the equilibrium and response properties of solids. In practical first-principles calculations, these derivatives are obtained via Wannier interpolation of position and related composite matrices. These matrices are initially evaluated on a coarse k-point grid using finite-difference approximations and then interpolated to a dense grid. The accuracy of the finite-difference approximation directly impacts the convergence and reliability of the result. In this work, we present two key improvements to the finite-difference calculation of position and composite operators for Wannier interpolation. First, we formulate a translationally equivariant scheme that preserves the underlying symmetries of the system and significantly reduces finite-difference errors. Second, we introduce a higher-order finite-difference approach that yields a more accurate approximation of the k-space derivatives by systematically increasing the convergence rate. From a real-space perspective, these improvements correspond to better approximations of the position operator at the locations of the Wannier functions. We also present a generalization of the finite-difference scheme, which may reduce the number of finite-difference points while maintaining accuracy. We demonstrate the effectiveness of our methods by applying them to the calculation of Wannier centers and spreads, electric polarization, off-diagonal position matrix elements, orbital magnetization, and spin Hall conductivity. Our results demonstrate significant reductions in finite-difference errors, elimination of symmetry-violating errors, and improved convergence with respect to k-point sampling. These methods have been implemented in the open-source packages and can be readily adopted in other Wannier-based codes with minimal computational overhead.

</details>
